Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
A Judge Writing to Another Judge: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Explains the ruling on inter-judicial correspondence by istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inter-judicial correspondence, explain why qiyas would prohibit it, and apply istihsan to justify its permissibility in non-hadd cases.

Core Idea
A judge's letter is weaker than speech, but istihsan permits it for necessity.

Writing to another judge seems weak and prone to forgery, but the Hanafi school carves out an exception: it is allowed in all cases except hudud and qisas. This ruling is not based on analogy (qiyas) but on juristic preference (istihsan) driven by practical need and the precedent of Ali (ra). Let's work through why qiyas says no and why istihsan says yes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling by qiyas
First, note what is being judged: a judge writing a letter to another judge. By strict analogy (qiyas), this should be invalid because the judge's own oral statement is not accepted in another court — so his writing, which risks forgery, is even weaker.
The asl (original case) is an oral statement from judge to judge.
2
Locate the exception and its basis
The matn states the ruling is allowed by istihsan. Identify the two evidences: (1) the athar (practice) of Ali (ra), and (2) the need (hajah) that cannot be fulfilled by testimony upon testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada).
Istihsan is a licensed departure from qiyas due to a stronger evidence or necessity.
3
Trace the scope of the exception
The exception covers everything except hadd and qisas. The passage lists several categories: debt, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, will, etc. Understand why hadd/qisas are excluded: the higher probability of doubt (shubha) in such cases demands the strongest proof.
The exclusion shows that even with istihsan, the limit is drawn where doubt would cause irreparable harm.
4
Note the authority and tarjih
The text attributes the permissibility to a narration from Imam Muhammad. It then says 'upon it are the later scholars and by it fatwa is given'. Identify this as the mufta bihi (operative) position, coming from the later authorities (fatawa mutakhkhirin) due to practicality.
This demonstrates how ikhtilaf within the school often settles on the more practical view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره
هذا أيضاً من أحكام القضاء غير أنه لا يتحقق في الوجود إلا بقاضيين فهو كالمركب بالنسبة لما قبله. كذا في فتح القدير وهو أولى مما ذكر الشارح من أن هذا الباب ليس من كتاب القضاء لأنه إما نقل شهادة أو نقل حكم، وكل ذلك ليس منه وإنما أورده فيه لأنه من عمل القضاة فكان ذكره فيه أنسب. وحيث كان من عملهم فهو منه فكيف ينفيه. والمراد بغيره ما ذكره في هذا الباب من قوله وتقضي المرأة إلى آخره. قوله: (يكتب القاضي إلى القاضي في غير حد وقود) أي استحساناً، والقياس أن لا يجوز لأن كتابته لا تكون أقوى من عبارته، وهو لو أخبر القاضي الآخر في محله لم يعمل بخبره فكتابته أولى لأنه قد يزور. جوزناه لأثر علي رضي الله عنه وللحاجة ولا يستغنى عنه بالشهادة على الشهادة لأن القاضي يحتاج فيها إلى تعديل الأصول وقد يتعذر ذلك، ولم يجز في الحدود والقصاص لما فيه من الشبهة بزيادة الاحتمال. ويدخل تحت قوله في غير حد وقود كل شيء من الدين والنكاح والطلاق والشفعة والوكالة والوصية والإيصاء والموت والوراثة والقتل إذا كان [المال؟] والنسب من الحي والميت والغصب والأمانة المجحودة من وديعة ومضاربة وعارية والأعيان منقولاً أو عقاراً وهو المروي عن محمد وعليه المتأخرون وبه يفتى للضرورة.
Chapter: The Judge's Writing to Another Judge and Others. This is also among the rulings of judiciary, except that it is not realized in existence except with two judges, so it is composite relative to what preceded. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and it is more appropriate than what the commentator mentioned that this chapter is not from the book of judiciary because it is either transmission of testimony or transmission of a ruling, and all that is not from it, but he included it because it is from the work of judges, so its mention in it is more suitable. And the meaning of 'others' is what he mentioned in this chapter from his statement 'and the woman gives judgment' to its end. His statement: (The judge writes to the judge in other than hadd [and] qisas) meaning as istihsan, and by qiyas it is not permissible because his writing is not stronger than his speech, and if he had informed the other judge in his court, he would not act upon his information; his writing is even less likely to be accepted because it may be forged. We allowed it due to the athar of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) and for need, and the need cannot be fulfilled by testimony upon testimony because the judge needs therein to adjust the original witnesses and that may be difficult. And it is not allowed in hadd and qisas because of the doubt due to increased probability. And under his statement 'in other than hadd [and] qisas' enters everything: debt, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, will, bequest, death, inheritance, murder when [involving property?], lineage from the living and dead, appropriation (ghasb), denied trust from deposit, partnership, loan, and chattels movable or immovable. This is the narration from Muhammad and upon it are the later scholars, and by it fatwa is given due to necessity.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the chapter of a judge writing to another judge. The author begins by justifying why this chapter is included in the book of judiciary, noting that it involves two judges and is part of their work. The matn states: 'The judge writes to the judge in other than hadd and qisas.' The commentator explains that this ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), while qiyas would prohibit it because a written document is weaker than oral testimony and susceptible to forgery. The school allows it because of the precedent (athar) of Ali (ra) and practical necessity, as it cannot be replaced by testimony upon testimony (shahada 'ala al-shahada), which requires adjusting original witnesses and is often unavailable. This permissibility is limited to non-hadd and non-qisas cases due to the high potential for doubt (shubha) in those severe penalties. The text then lists the types of cases that fall under this permission: all matters of debt, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, will, bequest, death, inheritance, murder (when it involves property, i.e., diyat not qisas), lineage, appropriation (ghasb), denied trusts like deposits, partnerships, loans, and chattels movable or immovable. This list shows the scope of the ruling. The commentator notes that this is the narration from Imam Muhammad (al-Shaybani) and is the relied-upon position adopted by later scholars, and fatwa is given by it due to necessity.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another, typically containing testimony or a ruling that the recipient judge may act upon.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence such as need, custom, or public interest.
شهادة على الشهادة (shahada 'ala al-shahada): Testimony given on the basis of other witnesses' testimony, i.e., witnesses testifying about what they heard from the original witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary basis for the Hanafi ruling that a judge may write to another judge in non-hadd matters?
A) Direct Quranic command
B) Qiyas on oral testimony
C) Istihsan based on the practice of Ali (ra) and necessity
D) Consensus of all scholars

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law permits a judge's letter to another judge in all non-hadd cases by istihsan, overriding qiyas, due to the precedent of Ali and practical necessity.

The Judge's Letter: Permissibility by Istihsan
Opening of the chapter on judge writing to judge: basic ruling and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a judge writing to another judge and explain why it is permitted by istihsan despite the qiyas prohibiting it.

Core Idea
A judge's written communication is accepted due to need and the precedent of Ali, overriding the qiyas that would require oral testimony.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational ruling that a judge may write to another judge in all matters except hadd and qisas. The author of Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that this is an exception to qiyas, allowed by istihsan. We will trace the proof from the athar of Ali and understand why hadd and qisas are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
What is the rule? The judge writes to another judge in matters other than hadd and qisas.
This is the primary statement.
2
Understand the qiyas
What would qiyas dictate? If a judge personally informs another judge outside his jurisdiction, his oral report is not accepted. By analogy, his writing would be even less reliable, so it should also be rejected.
Qiyas would prohibit it.
3
Recognize the istihsan
Why is it allowed despite qiyas? Due to istihsan, based on the athar of Ali and practical need. The athar provides a stronger evidence that overrides qiyas.
Istihsan is a Hanafi source.
4
Note the exception
What is excluded? Hadd punishments and qisas (retaliation) are not included due to heightened uncertainty (shubha).
These are serious matters requiring absolute certainty.

يكتب القاضي إلى القاضي في غير حد وقود فإن شهدا على خصم حاضر حكم

باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره هذا أيضاً من أحكام القضاء غير أنه لا يتحقق في الوجود إلا بقاضيين فهو كالمركب بالنسبة لما قبله. كذا في فتح القدير وهو أولى مما ذكر الشارح من أن هذا الباب ليس من كتاب القضاء لأنه إما نقل شهادة أو نقل حكم، وكل ذلك ليس منه وإنما أورده فيه لأنه من عمل القضاة فكان ذكره فيه أنسب .اهـ وحيث كان من عملهم فهو منه فكيف ينفيه. والمراد بغيره ما ذكره في هذا الباب من قوله وتقضي تحت قوله في غير حد وقود كل شيء من الدين والنكاح والطلاق والشفعة والوكالة والوصية والإيصاء والموت والوراثة والقتل إذا كان المال والنسب من الحي والميت والغصب والأمانة المجحودة من وديعة ومضاربة وعارية والأعيان منقولاً أو عقاراً وهو المروي عن محمد وعليه المتأخرون وبه يفتى للضرورة، وفي ظاهر الرواية لا يجوز في المنقول للحاجة إلى الإشارة إليها عند الدعوى والشهادة، وعن الإمام الثاني تجويزه في العبد لغلبة الإباق فيه لا في الأمة، وعنه تجويزه في الكل. وفي البزازية : والمتقدمون لم يأخذوا بقول الإمام الثاني، وعمل الفقهاء اليوم على التجويز في الكل للحاجة . قال الإمام الأسبيجابي : وعليه الفتوى ولو جاء المدعي من القاضي برسول ثقة مأمون عدل إلى قاض آخر لا يقبل لأنه لا يزيد على أن يأتي القاضي بنفسه ويخبر وهو في غير ولايته كواحد من الرعايا بخلاف كتابه لأنه كالخطاب من مجلس قضائه دلت التفرقة على مسألتين : الأولى بلدة فيها قاضيان حضر أحدهما مجلس القاضي الآخر وأخبر بحادثة لا يجوز له أن يعمل بخبره وحده ولو كتب إليه بشرطه له العمل به وكذا لو حضر قاضيان في مصر ليس فيه مجلس قاض أو أحدهما قاض فيه والآخر ليس بقاض فيه لا يعمل بخبر من ليس بقاض فيه لعدم الولاية كقاض ببخارى التقى مع قاض بخوارزم وأخبره بحادثة حكم فيها بخارى لا يعمل بإخباره قاضي خوارزم .اهـ. وقد ذكر قاضيخان في فتاواه مسائل : الأولى طلب من القاضي أن يسمع شهوده على الإبراء أو إيفاء الدين ويكتب له كتاباً بذلك خوفاً من رب الدين أن يدعي عليه إذا ذهب إليه، لم يكتب في قول أبي يوسف، ويكتب في قول محمد. الثانية لو كان صاحب الدين حاضراً وطلب من القاضي أن يسأله فإذا أنكر برهن ليكتب له لم يسأله إجماعاً وهذه حجة على محمد في السابقة الثالثة امرأة جاءت إلى القاضي وقالت طلقني زوجي فلان ثلاثاً وتزوجت بأخر بعد العدة وأخاف إنكاره فاسأله، فإن أنكر القاضي إجماعاً سأله وهي حجة على أبي يوسف. الرابعة ادعى أنه مشتر داراً لها شفيع سلمها وهي في بلد كذا وطلب أن يسمع شهوده ويكتب لا يكتب، وقال محمد يكتب في هذه المسائل كلها احتياطاً احترازاً عن تضييع الحقوق وأجمعوا على أن المديون أو المشتري أو المرأة لو قال إن صاحب الدين والشفيع والزوج قد تعرض لي فيما ادعى فاسمع شهودي فإن القاضي يسمع ويكتب اهـ. أطلق القاضي فأفاد أن قاضي مصر يكتب إلى قاضي مصر آخر وإلى قاضي السواد والرستاق ولا يكتب قاضي الرستاق إلى قاضي مصر. كذا في السراج الوهاج معزياً إلى الينابيع. ثم قال : وإنما يقبل إذا كان بينهما مسيرة ثلاثة أيام فصاعداً، أما إذا كان أقل من ذلك لا يقبل. وفي نوادر ابن هشام : إذا كان في المصر قاضيان جاز كتابهما

برهنت النهر : وعندي أنه لا تنافي بينهما بوجه إذ المنفي في كلام الشارح كونه قضاء والمثبت في الفتح كونه من أحكام القضاء ولا يلزم منه أن يكون قضاء نعم كونه من أحكامه أدخل في كتاب القضاء قوله : (ليس فيه مجلس قاض قال الرملي : أي ليسا بقاضيين فيه .
قوله : (ولا) يكتب قاضي الرستاق إلى قاضي مصر) قال في منح الغفار بعد نقله الخلاف في المسألة أقول : الظاهر أن الخلاف بينهم في هذه المسألة مبني على الخلاف في أن المصر هل هو شرط
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Chapter of the Judge's Letter to Another Judge and Other Matters. The judge writes to the judge in other than hadd and qisas, and if they (two witnesses) testify against a present opponent, he judges. This chapter is also from the rulings of judiciary, except that it does not occur in reality except with two judges, so it is like a composite relative to what came before. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and it is better than what the commentator mentioned that this chapter is not from the book of judiciary because it is either transferring testimony or transferring a ruling, and all of that is not from it; rather he brought it here because it is from the work of judges, so mentioning it here is more appropriate. Since it is from their work, then it is from it, so how can he negate it? And what is meant by 'and other matters' is what he mentioned in this chapter from his statement 'and the woman judges' etc. His statement: 'The judge writes to the judge in other than hadd and qisas' meaning, by istihsan, and by qiyas it is not permissible because his writing is not stronger than his statement, and if he informed the other judge in his place, he would not act on his report, so his writing is more likely to be forged. We allowed it due to the athar of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and due to need; it cannot be dispensed with by testimony upon testimony because the judge needs in that to verify the witnesses and that may be difficult. And it is not permitted in hadd and qisas due to the shubha of increased likelihood. And under his statement 'in other than hadd and qisas' enters everything of debt, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, will, testament, death, inheritance, murder if it involves property, lineage from the living and dead, usurpation, trust property denied—such as deposit, partnership, loan—and items, movable or immovable. This is the narration from Muhammad, and the later scholars follow it, and fatwa is given by it due to necessity. In the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), it is not permissible for movable property due to the need to point to it at time of claim and testimony. And from Imam the Second (Abu Yusuf) is the permissibility in a slave due to the prevalence of his running away, not in a female slave, and from him permissibility in all. In al-Bazzaziyya: The earlier scholars did not adopt the statement of Imam the Second, but the practice of the jurists today is on permissibility in all due to need. Imam al-Asbijabi said: Fatwa is upon it. If the plaintiff comes from the judge with a trustworthy, reliable, just messenger to another judge, it is not accepted because it is no more than the judge coming himself and informing, and he is outside his jurisdiction like one of the subjects, unlike his letter because it is like an address from his judicial council. This distinction implies two issues: First, a town with two judges; one of them attended the other's judicial council and informed about an incident; it is not permissible for him to act on his report alone, but if he wrote to him with its condition, he would act on it. Likewise, if two judges were present in a city where there is no judicial council, or one of them is a judge there and the other is not a judge there, he does not act on the report of the one not a judge there due to lack of jurisdiction—like a judge in Bukhara meeting a judge in Khwarazm and informing him of an incident he ruled on in Bukhara; the judge of Khwarazm does not act on his report. Qadhikhan mentioned in his Fatawa issues: First: Someone asked the judge to hear his witnesses on the discharge or payment of debt and write him a letter on that, fearing that the creditor might claim against him if he goes to him; he does not write according to Abu Yusuf, but writes according to Muhammad. Second: If the creditor was present and asked the judge to question him, and if he denied to produce proof to write for him, he does not question him by consensus; this is evidence against Muhammad in the previous issue. Third: A woman came to the judge and said: 'My husband so-and-so divorced me three times, and I married another after the 'iddah, and I fear his denial; ask him.' And if he denies, by consensus the judge asks him; this is evidence against Abu Yusuf. Fourth: He claimed that he bought a house which has a pre-emptor who has waived his right, and it is in such a town, and he asked to hear his witnesses and write; he does not write. Muhammad said: He writes in all these issues as a precaution against wasting rights. And they all agreed that if the debtor, the buyer, or the woman says that the creditor, pre-emptor, or husband has interfered with him in what he claimed, then hear my witnesses, the judge hears and writes. This statement about the judge is absolute, so it indicates that the judge of Egypt writes to another judge of Egypt and to the judge of the Sawad and the villages, but the judge of the villages does not write to the judge of Egypt. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, citing al-Yanabi'. Then he said: It is only accepted if the distance between them is three days' journey or more; if it is less, it is not accepted. In Nawadir Ibn Hisham: If there are two judges in one city, their letters are permissible. Burhan al-Nahr? And in my view, there is no contradiction between them at all, since what is negated in the commentator's speech is its being judiciary, and what is affirmed in al-Fath is its being from the rulings of judiciary; it does not require it to be judiciary; indeed being from its rulings makes it included in the book of judiciary. His statement: 'There is no judicial council in it.' Al-Ramli said: Meaning they are not both judges in it. His statement: 'The judge of the village does not write to the judge of Egypt.' He said in Minah al-Ghaffar, after quoting the disagreement on the issue, I say: It appears that the disagreement among them on this issue is based on the disagreement on whether the city is a condition.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the rules of a judge writing to another judge. It begins by stating the ruling: a judge may write to another judge in all matters except hadd (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) despite the qiyas (analogy) that would prohibit it, because a judge's oral statement in another's jurisdiction is not accepted, so his writing would be even less trustworthy. However, the ruling is permitted due to the athar (tradition) of Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) and due to practical need, as the alternative (testimony upon testimony) requires verifying original witnesses, which may be difficult. The exception for hadd and qisas is due to the heightened suspicion (shubha) in such cases. The passage then details what falls under 'other than hadd and qisas': debts, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, wills, death, inheritance, murder involving property, lineage, usurpation, and various trusts and properties—both movable and immovable. This is the narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, and later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) adopted it for fatwa due to necessity. However, according to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), it is not allowed for movable property because of the need to identify them in claims. There is also a difference of opinion from Imams Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on specific cases. The text then discusses that a messenger cannot replace a written letter because the letter carries judicial authority from the judge's council. It also gives examples from Qadhikhan's Fatawa about when a judge should or should not write, with disagreements between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Finally, it addresses that the judge of a major city (like Egypt) can write to judges of other regions, but a village judge cannot write to a city judge, and the acceptance requires a distance of three days' travel. The passage ends with a note on the disagreement about whether a city (misr) is a condition for this.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another regarding a legal matter, serving as a means of transferring a case or evidence between jurisdictions.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence (such as an athar) or necessity, leading to a more equitable ruling.
حد وقود (hadd wa qisas): Hadd refers to fixed punishments (hudud) prescribed by Shariah; qisas is retaliation for murder or bodily harm. These are excluded from permissibility due to heightened caution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which cases is a judge allowed to write to another judge?
A) Only in criminal cases
B) In all cases including hadd and qisas
C) In all cases except hadd and qisas
D) Only in cases involving immovable property

Key Takeaway
The basic ruling: a judge's letter is permissible (mubah) by istihsan, except for hadd and qisas.

Qada — Judiciary

Scope and Intra-Hanafi Disagreements on the Judge's Letter
Detailed scope and intra-Hanafi differences on what the judge may write about

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the matters included in the judge's letter, explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on movable property, and state the mufta bihi (operative fatwa).

Core Idea
The Hanafi school evolved from restricting the judge's letter to immovable property to permitting it for all matters due to necessity.

In this lesson, we delve into the detailed enumeration of cases where a judge may write to another judge. We will see the initial restriction in Zahir al-Riwayah excluding movable property, the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and how later jurists adopted permissibility for all cases due to necessity. We also consider related conditions like distance and the status of the judge's city.

Guided Path
1
Identify the included matters
List all the cases that fall under 'other than hadd and qisas': debts, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, wills, death, inheritance, murder involving property, lineage, usurpation, trusts, and movable/immovable items.
The list is comprehensive.
2
Understand the Zahir al-Riwayah restriction
According to the apparent narration from Muhammad, movable property is excluded because it requires pointing to specific items during claims and testimony, which is difficult.
This is the default position.
3
Examine the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad later
Abu Yusuf allowed for slaves but not female slaves; later he allowed all. Muhammad eventually allowed all. The earlier scholars did not accept Abu Yusuf's view, but later scholars adopted permissibility for all due to need.
The development over time.
4
Apply the conditions for acceptance
The letter is only accepted if the distance between the two judges is three days' journey or more, and the writing judge must be from a city (misr) or at least have proper jurisdiction. A village judge cannot write to a city judge.
These conditions are from the later part of the passage.

يكتب القاضي إلى القاضي في غير حد وقود فإن شهدا على خصم حاضر حكم

قوله : (ولا) يكتب قاضي الرستاق إلى قاضي مصر) قال في منح الغفار بعد نقله الخلاف في المسألة أقول : الظاهر أن الخلاف بينهم في هذه المسألة مبني على الخلاف في أن المصر هل هو شرط
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. Chapter of the Judge's Letter to Another Judge and Other Matters. The judge writes to the judge in other than hadd and qisas, and if they (two witnesses) testify against a present opponent, he judges. This chapter is also from the rulings of judiciary, except that it does not occur in reality except with two judges, so it is like a composite relative to what came before. Thus in Fath al-Qadir, and it is better than what the commentator mentioned that this chapter is not from the book of judiciary because it is either transferring testimony or transferring a ruling, and all of that is not from it; rather he brought it here because it is from the work of judges, so mentioning it here is more appropriate. Since it is from their work, then it is from it, so how can he negate it? And what is meant by 'and other matters' is what he mentioned in this chapter from his statement 'and the woman judges' etc. His statement: 'The judge writes to the judge in other than hadd and qisas' meaning, by istihsan, and by qiyas it is not permissible because his writing is not stronger than his statement, and if he informed the other judge in his place, he would not act on his report, so his writing is more likely to be forged. We allowed it due to the athar of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, and due to need; it cannot be dispensed with by testimony upon testimony because the judge needs in that to verify the witnesses and that may be difficult. And it is not permitted in hadd and qisas due to the shubha of increased likelihood. And under his statement 'in other than hadd and qisas' enters everything of debt, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, will, testament, death, inheritance, murder if it involves property, lineage from the living and dead, usurpation, trust property denied—such as deposit, partnership, loan—and items, movable or immovable. This is the narration from Muhammad, and the later scholars follow it, and fatwa is given by it due to necessity. In the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), it is not permissible for movable property due to the need to point to it at time of claim and testimony. And from Imam the Second (Abu Yusuf) is the permissibility in a slave due to the prevalence of his running away, not in a female slave, and from him permissibility in all. In al-Bazzaziyya: The earlier scholars did not adopt the statement of Imam the Second, but the practice of the jurists today is on permissibility in all due to need. Imam al-Asbijabi said: Fatwa is upon it. If the plaintiff comes from the judge with a trustworthy, reliable, just messenger to another judge, it is not accepted because it is no more than the judge coming himself and informing, and he is outside his jurisdiction like one of the subjects, unlike his letter because it is like an address from his judicial council. This distinction implies two issues: First, a town with two judges; one of them attended the other's judicial council and informed about an incident; it is not permissible for him to act on his report alone, but if he wrote to him with its condition, he would act on it. Likewise, if two judges were present in a city where there is no judicial council, or one of them is a judge there and the other is not a judge there, he does not act on the report of the one not a judge there due to lack of jurisdiction—like a judge in Bukhara meeting a judge in Khwarazm and informing him of an incident he ruled on in Bukhara; the judge of Khwarazm does not act on his report. Qadhikhan mentioned in his Fatawa issues: First: Someone asked the judge to hear his witnesses on the discharge or payment of debt and write him a letter on that, fearing that the creditor might claim against him if he goes to him; he does not write according to Abu Yusuf, but writes according to Muhammad. Second: If the creditor was present and asked the judge to question him, and if he denied to produce proof to write for him, he does not question him by consensus; this is evidence against Muhammad in the previous issue. Third: A woman came to the judge and said: 'My husband so-and-so divorced me three times, and I married another after the 'iddah, and I fear his denial; ask him.' And if he denies, by consensus the judge asks him; this is evidence against Abu Yusuf. Fourth: He claimed that he bought a house which has a pre-emptor who has waived his right, and it is in such a town, and he asked to hear his witnesses and write; he does not write. Muhammad said: He writes in all these issues as a precaution against wasting rights. And they all agreed that if the debtor, the buyer, or the woman says that the creditor, pre-emptor, or husband has interfered with him in what he claimed, then hear my witnesses, the judge hears and writes. This statement about the judge is absolute, so it indicates that the judge of Egypt writes to another judge of Egypt and to the judge of the Sawad and the villages, but the judge of the villages does not write to the judge of Egypt. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, citing al-Yanabi'. Then he said: It is only accepted if the distance between them is three days' journey or more; if it is less, it is not accepted. In Nawadir Ibn Hisham: If there are two judges in one city, their letters are permissible. Burhan al-Nahr? And in my view, there is no contradiction between them at all, since what is negated in the commentator's speech is its being judiciary, and what is affirmed in al-Fath is its being from the rulings of judiciary; it does not require it to be judiciary; indeed being from its rulings makes it included in the book of judiciary. His statement: 'There is no judicial council in it.' Al-Ramli said: Meaning they are not both judges in it. His statement: 'The judge of the village does not write to the judge of Egypt.' He said in Minah al-Ghaffar, after quoting the disagreement on the issue, I say: It appears that the disagreement among them on this issue is based on the disagreement on whether the city is a condition.
Source pages p. 3

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, addresses the rules of a judge writing to another judge. It begins by stating the ruling: a judge may write to another judge in all matters except hadd (fixed punishments) and qisas (retaliation). This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) despite the qiyas (analogy) that would prohibit it, because a judge's oral statement in another's jurisdiction is not accepted, so his writing would be even less trustworthy. However, the ruling is permitted due to the athar (tradition) of Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) and due to practical need, as the alternative (testimony upon testimony) requires verifying original witnesses, which may be difficult. The exception for hadd and qisas is due to the heightened suspicion (shubha) in such cases. The passage then details what falls under 'other than hadd and qisas': debts, marriage, divorce, pre-emption, agency, wills, death, inheritance, murder involving property, lineage, usurpation, and various trusts and properties—both movable and immovable. This is the narration from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, and later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) adopted it for fatwa due to necessity. However, according to the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah), it is not allowed for movable property because of the need to identify them in claims. There is also a difference of opinion from Imams Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on specific cases. The text then discusses that a messenger cannot replace a written letter because the letter carries judicial authority from the judge's council. It also gives examples from Qadhikhan's Fatawa about when a judge should or should not write, with disagreements between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Finally, it addresses that the judge of a major city (like Egypt) can write to judges of other regions, but a village judge cannot write to a city judge, and the acceptance requires a distance of three days' travel. The passage ends with a note on the disagreement about whether a city (misr) is a condition for this.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي (kitab al-qadi ila al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another regarding a legal matter.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known narration from the founding imams, especially Muhammad al-Shaybani.
متعاقبون (muta'akhkhirun): Later Hanafi jurists who gave weight to necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the later Hanafi fatwa, which of the following is true about the judge's letter?
A) It is allowed only for immovable property.
B) It is allowed for all matters except hadd and qisas, including movable property.
C) It is allowed for all matters including hadd and qisas.
D) It is allowed only if a messenger is used instead.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school evolved to permit a judge's letter for all matters except hadd and qisas, with the later fatwa overriding the initial restriction on movable property.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
8 min
The Ruling of a Judge Writing to Another Judge: From Impermissibility to Permissibility by Necessity
Establishes the ruling of a judge writing to another judge, including differences and later practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the original Hanafi ruling on a judge writing to another judge regarding movable property, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the later mufta bihi position with its proof.

Core Idea
A judge's written letter carries judicial authority; an oral messenger does not, because the judge outside his jurisdiction is like a private subject.

In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of a judge writing to another judge involves a key distinction between written communication and oral testimony. The original transmitted position (zahir al-riwayah) forbade writing about movable property, but later jurists permitted it for all matters due to necessity. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the intra-Hanafi difference, and the reasoning behind why a written letter is authoritative while an oral report from a messenger is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the original ruling
The act is a judge writing to another judge about a case. The original transmitted position (riwayah) is that it is not permissible (la yajuz) for movable property.
Movable property requires detailed description; hence impermissibility.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam al-Thani (the second imam) introduced a permissibility for slaves (due to frequent running away), and another narration permits for everything.
Imam al-Thani likely refers to Abu Yusuf or Muhammad al-Shaybani.
3
Trace the later tarjih and mufta bihi
The earlier scholars did not adopt the permissive view, but later jurists (mutakhkhirun) adopted permissibility for all things due to necessity; Imam al-Asbijabi confirms the fatwa.
The shift is based on need (hajah), not a change in the textual ruling.
4
Understand the proof of the distinction
The proof: if a claimant comes with a trustworthy messenger, the messenger's report is not accepted because the messenger is like a private individual outside his jurisdiction. A written letter, however, is like an address from the judge's court, thus carries authority.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli) based on the nature of judicial authority.
5
Apply the distinction to subsidiary issues
Two issues: (a) two judges in one town – one cannot act on the other's oral report unless written; (b) a judge and a non-judge – the non-judge's report is rejected; cross-jurisdiction oral reports are invalid.
The example of Bukhara and Khwarazm illustrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره الرواية لا يجوز في المنقول للحاجة إلى الإشارة إليها عند الدعوى والشهادة، وعن الإمام الثاني تجويزه في العبد لغلبة الإباق فيه لا في الأمة، وعنه تجويزه في الكل. وفي البزازية : والمتقدمون لم يأخذوا بقول الإمام الثاني، وعمل الفقهاء اليوم على التجويز في الكل للحاجة . قال الإمام الأسبيجابي : وعليه الفتوى ولو جاء المدعي من القاضي برسول ثقة مأمون عدل إلى قاض آخر لا يقبل لأنه لا يزيد على أن يأتي القاضي بنفسه ويخبر وهو في غير ولايته كواحد من الرعايا بخلاف كتابه لأنه كالخطاب من مجلس قضائه دلت التفرقة على مسألتين : الأولى بلدة فيها قاضيان حضر أحدهما مجلس القاضي الآخر وأخبر بحادثة لا يجوز له أن يعمل بخبره وحده ولو كتب إليه بشرطه له العمل به وكذا لو حضر قاضيان في مصر ليس فيه مجلس قاض أو أحدهما قاض فيه والآخر ليس بقاض فيه لا يعمل بخبر من ليس بقاض فيه لعدم الولاية كقاض ببخارى التقى مع قاض بخوارزم وأخبره بحادثة حكم فيها بخارى لا يعمل بإخباره قاضي خوارزم .اهـ.
Book of Hawala, chapter of the judge writing to another judge and other matters. The transmitted position: it is not permissible for movable property due to the need to point to it during claim and testimony. And from Imam al-Thani: permissibility for a slave due to prevalence of running away, not for a female slave. And from him: permissibility for everything. In al-Bazzaziyya: the earlier scholars did not adopt the saying of Imam al-Thani, and the jurists today act upon permissibility for everything due to need. Imam al-Asbijabi said: And upon it is the fatwa. And if the claimant comes from the judge with a trustworthy, reliable, just messenger to another judge, it is not accepted because it is no more than the judge coming himself and informing, and he is outside his jurisdiction like one of the subjects, unlike his writing because it is like an address from his court of justice. The distinction indicates two issues: First: a town in which there are two judges; one of them attended the court of the other judge and informed of an incident. It is not permissible for him to act upon his report alone. If he wrote to him with its condition, he would act upon it. Similarly, if two judges are present in a city that has no court judge, or one of them is a judge there and the other is not a judge there, the report of the one who is not a judge is not acted upon due to lack of jurisdiction, like a judge of Bukhara meeting a judge of Khwarazm and informing him of an incident he judged in Bukhara; the judge of Khwarazm does not act upon his information.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (dalil aqli) discussing the permissibility of a judge writing to another judge. The original ruling (zahir al-riwayah) forbids writing about movable property because identification is needed for litigation. Imam al-Thani (likely Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) permitted writing for slaves due to frequent running away, but not for female slaves. A further narration permits for all things. Later jurists (mutakhkhirun) adopted the permissive view due to necessity. The proof: a messenger's oral report is not accepted because the messenger is like a private individual outside his jurisdiction and cannot substitute for the judge's written address. The passage then derives two subsidiary issues: (1) if two judges are in the same town, one cannot act on the other's oral report unless written; (2) if one is a judge and the other is not, the report of the non-judge is rejected entirely. The example of Bukhara and Khwarazm illustrates that judges from different jurisdictions cannot rely on oral reports.

Key Terms
الرواية (al-riwayah): The transmitted position (from the founding imams), likely referring to the zahir al-riwayah.
الإمام الثاني (al-Imam al-Thani): Likely Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani or Abu Yusuf; contextually, it is the imam whose ruling deviated from the original.
الكتاب الحكمي (al-kitab al-hukmi): Not explicitly in this lesson but in the next. Here 'كتابه' refers to the judge's written letter.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a messenger's oral report not accepted from another judge?
A) Because the messenger might be lying.
B) Because the messenger is outside the judge's jurisdiction and is like an ordinary subject.
C) Because the messenger is not a judge himself.
D) Because the matter requires physical inspection.

Key Takeaway
A judge's written letter to another judge carries judicial authority; an oral report does not, and the Hanafi ruling shifted from impermissibility to permissibility for all things due to necessity.

Qada — Judiciary

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Judicial Documentation
Four cases of judicial documentation with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in each of the four cases and explain the consensus exceptions.

Core Idea
The judge's duty to pre-record evidence depends on the risk of rights being wasted—Muhammad expands, Abu Yusuf restricts.

In his Fatawa, Qadikhan presents four scenarios where a party seeks judicial documentation out of fear. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree on whether the judge should write. Their ikhtilaf reveals a tension between procedural strictness and precautionary preservation of rights. Understanding each case sharpens your ability to apply Hanafi judicial discretion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios
Read each case: debtor fearing future claim, creditor present, divorced woman, purchaser fearing preemptor. Note the request and the fear.
2
Extract Abu Yusuf's ruling per case
Abu Yusuf only writes in the second and third scenarios (by consensus in those actual situations). In the first and fourth, he refuses.
He is the restrictor.
3
Extract Muhammad's ruling per case
Muhammad writes in all four cases as a precaution (ihtiyatan) to avoid wasting rights.
He is the expander.
4
Note the consensus exceptions
In the second and third cases, the text says the judge acts by consensus (ajma'u). In the second, the judge does not question; in the third, the judge does question. These show that when the opponent is present and the claim is specific, the duty is clear.
These consensus points argue against the previous positions.
5
Synthesize the principle
Muhammad's principle: ihtiyat (caution) to safeguard rights. Abu Yusuf's implicit principle: the judge should not preemptively document unless there is an immediate confrontation (as in cases 2 and 3). The final consensus: if the opponent has already confronted, documentation is obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ذكر قاضيخان في فتاواه مسائل : الأولى طلب من القاضي أن يسمع شهوده على الإبراء أو إيفاء الدين ويكتب له كتاباً بذلك خوفاً من رب الدين أن يدعي عليه إذا ذهب إليه، لم يكتب في قول أبي يوسف، ويكتب في قول محمد. الثانية لو كان صاحب الدين حاضراً وطلب من القاضي أن يسأله فإذا أنكر برهن ليكتب له لم يسأله إجماعاً وهذه حجة على محمد في السابقة الثالثة امرأة جاءت إلى القاضي وقالت طلقني زوجي فلان ثلاثاً وتزوجت بأخر بعد العدة وأخاف إنكاره فاسأله، فإن أنكر القاضي إجماعاً سأله وهي حجة على أبي يوسف. الرابعة ادعى أنه مشتر داراً لها شفيع سلمها وهي في بلد كذا وطلب أن يسمع شهوده ويكتب لا يكتب، وقال محمد يكتب في هذه المسائل كلها احتياطاً احترازاً عن تضييع الحقوق وأجمعوا على أن المديون أو المشتري أو المرأة لو قال إن صاحب الدين والشفيع والزوج قد تعرض لي فيما ادعى فاسمع شهودي فإن القاضي يسمع ويكتب اهـ.
Qadikhan mentioned in his Fatawa issues: First: [A person] asked the judge to hear his witnesses regarding a discharge or payment of debt and to write him a letter about that out of fear that the creditor would claim against him when he goes to him. He does not write according to the saying of Abu Yusuf, and he writes according to Muhammad's saying. Second: If the creditor is present and [the debtor] asks the judge to question him, and if he denies, [the debtor] presents proof so that [the judge] writes for him; [the judge] does not question him by consensus. And this is an argument against Muhammad in the previous [issue]. Third: A woman came to the judge and said: 'My husband so-and-so divorced me three times and I married another after the 'iddah, and I fear his denial. So question him.' If he denies, the judge questions him by consensus. And it is an argument against Abu Yusuf. Fourth: He claimed that he purchased a house that has a preemptor who has surrendered it, and it is in such a town, and he asked to hear his witnesses and write. He does not write. Muhammad said: He writes in all these cases out of caution, as a precaution against wasting rights. And they all agreed that if the debtor, purchaser, or woman says: 'The creditor, preemptor, or husband has confronted me regarding what I claim, so hear my witnesses,' then the judge hears and writes.
Source pages p. 4

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad) presented by Qadikhan. It lists four cases where a party requests a judge to document evidence. In the first case (debtor fearing future claim), Abu Yusuf refuses to write, Muhammad agrees. In the second, if the creditor is present and the debtor asks the judge to question him (with proof), all agree the judge does not question—this contradicts Muhammad's earlier permissiveness. In the third, a divorced woman fears her husband's denial; the judge must question him by consensus, which contradicts Abu Yusuf's position in similar cases. In the fourth, a house purchaser fears a preemptor; again, Abu Yusuf refuses, Muhammad writes. Muhammad's general principle is caution to avoid waste of rights. The passage also notes consensus: if the party says the opponent has already confronted them, the judge hears and writes immediately.

Key Terms
احتياطاً (ihtiyatan): As a precaution; Muhammad's principle to avoid wasting rights by documenting evidence early.
أجمعوا (ajma'u): They all agreed (by consensus of the Hanafi imams, specifically Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third case (divorced woman), what is the ruling by consensus?
A) The judge does not question the husband.
B) The judge questions the husband if he denies.
C) The wife must bring witnesses first.
D) The judge writes a letter to another judge.

Key Takeaway
The judge's discretion in pre‑trial documentation depends on the risk of rights being wasted, with Abu Yusuf restricting and Muhammad expanding the duty.

The Sijill and the Kitab Hukmi: Two Types of Judicial Writing
Definition and distinction of two types of judicial writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a sijill and a kitab hukmi, and explain the legal effect of each on the recipient judge.

Core Idea
A sijill binds; a kitab hukmi transfers testimony for new ijtihad.

When a judge communicates with another judge, the legal nature of the writing matters. Is it a final judgment to be enforced, or a transfer of testimony for the recipient to decide? This distinction—between a sijill and a kitab hukmi—determines whether the recipient must follow the original ruling or may exercise his own ijtihad. In this lesson, we parse the definitions and conditions from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Types
Locate in the text the two types of judicial writing: the sijill (record of judgment) and the kitab hukmi (judicial writing). Note their Arabic names.
The passage introduces them and gives their distinguishing features.
2
Define Each Type
Based on the text, write a precise definition for each. The sijill contains the judge's final judgment; the kitab hukmi transfers testimony for the recipient to judge.
The text says: 'السجل: الحجة التي فيها حكم القاضي' and 'الكتاب الحكمي: نقل الشهادة في الحقيقة'.
3
Compare Legal Effects
Contrast the binding force: the recipient of a sijill must enforce it even if contrary to his madhhab; the recipient of a kitab hukmi judges by his own opinion.
The key phrase: 'بخلاف السجل فإنه ليس له أن يخالفه' vs. 'يحكم المكتوب إليه برأيه وإن كان مخالفاً'.
4
Analyze the Condition of Opponent Presence
Understand that judgment is valid only if the opponent is present (either in person, by wakil, or musakhkhar). The text stresses this as a condition.
The passage uses 'خصم حاضر' and explains its meaning.
5
Note the Cross-Madhhab Dimension
The passage mentions that the ruling on judgment over absent differs: Hanafi prohibits, Shafi'i allows in one narration. This affects enforceability of writings.
This appears in the commentary: 'إذا كان القاضي شافعياً... في أظهر الروايتين'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

With testimony and he wrote his judgment, and that which is called a record (sijill). Otherwise he does not judge, and he wrote the testimony so that the one written to may judge by it, and that is the kitab hukmi (judicial writing), attributed to judgment in terms of what it leads to. And it is in reality a transfer of testimony because the writer did not judge by it but transferred it to the recipient to judge by it. Therefore the recipient judges by his own opinion even if contrary to the writer's opinion, unlike the sijill, where he may not contradict it. Also, if a judge judges by it and then it is transferred to him, he enacts it, unlike the kitab hukmi, which is not enforced if contrary to his madhhab, because the first considers that matter already judged and thus binding, while the second is initiating a judgment, so it is not permissible for him. Thus the commentator stated, and this indicates that if the judge over an absent person is a Hanafi, his judgment is not enforceable. This is beneficial because their saying that judgment over an absent person is enforceable in the more apparent of the two narrations if the judge is a Shafi’i. His statement: 'If they testify against a present opponent, he judges by the testimony because the evidence exists and the condition of judgment is met, which is the opponent's presence.' And the intended meaning of 'present opponent' is one who is an agent on behalf of the defendant or a deputy appointed by the judge for the absent party to hear the claim against him. Otherwise, if he meant the defendant himself, there would be no need for the letter to the other judge because the opponent is present before the judge and has been judged against. When he judges, he writes his judgment to the judge of the town where the principal resides to exact the right from him. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. His statement: 'And he wrote his judgment, which is called a record (sijill)' so that the incident is not forgotten over time and so that the letter serves as a reminder. Otherwise, there is no need to write the judgment because the opponent is present in person or by representation, except if it is assumed that he departed after judgment and denied it; then he writes so that his right is delivered to him or so that his judgment is enforced. In al-Misbah: al-sijill is the judge's book, plural sijillat. 'Asjaltu lirrajuli isjalan' means I wrote a book for him. 'Sajjala al-qadi' with tashdid means he judged, decreed, and recorded his judgment in the sijill. So the sijill is the document containing the judge's judgment. But this is in their technical usage. In our usage, the sijill is a large book in which people's cases and the judge's rulings are recorded. His statement: 'Otherwise he does not judge' means if the opponent is not present, he does not judge because judgment over an absent person is not permissible as is known. If a judge rendered a judgment and then it was transferred to him, he enacts it, unlike the kitab hukmi, which is not enforced if contrary to his madhhab because the first considers...
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage is a definition and ruling on two types of judicial writing. A 'sijill' is a record of a judgment already pronounced; the recipient judge must enforce it without modification, even if it conflicts with his own madhhab. A 'kitab hukmi' (judicial writing) is a transfer of testimony for the recipient judge to decide; he may judge according to his own ijtihad, even if it differs from the writer's opinion. The reason: the sijill reports an existing judgment, so it is binding; the kitab hukmi is merely evidence for a new judgment. The passage also explains the condition of the opponent's presence. The 'present opponent' can be an actual defendant, a representative (wakil), or a court-appointed deputy (musakhkhar) for the absent defendant. If the opponent is present, the judge can hear testimony and issue a judgment. If the opponent is absent, judgment is not permissible. However, if a prior judgment exists, it should be enforced. The commentator notes that Hanafi judgments over absent persons are not enforceable, but Shafi'i judges may enforce them according to one narration. The linguistic note on 'sijill' clarifies that it originally meant a document of judgment; later it came to mean a large register.

Key Terms
السجل (al-sijill): A document containing the judge's exact judgment; it is a record of what was already decided.
الكتاب الحكمي (al-kitab al-hukmi): A writing that transfers testimony for the recipient judge to decide; not a final judgment.
الخصم الحاضر (al-khasm al-hadir): The opponent who is present in court, either in person or through a representative (wakil or musakhkhar).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between a sijill and a kitab hukmi?
A) A sijill is oral, while kitab hukmi is written.
B) A sijill records a past judgment and is binding; a kitab hukmi transfers testimony for the recipient to judge.
C) A sijill can be modified by the recipient judge, while a kitab hukmi cannot.
D) A sijill is for criminal cases, kitab hukmi for civil.

Key Takeaway
The legal nature of a judicial writing determines whether it is binding (sijill) or merely a transfer for fresh ijtihad (kitab hukmi).

The Sijill and Kitab Hukmi: Binding vs. Discretionary Judicial Letters
Defines two judicial letter types and the dispute over witness conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a sijill and a kitab hukmi, state the conditions for their acceptance, and identify the mufta bihi position on the need for witnesses.

Core Idea
A sijill is binding; a kitab hukmi transmits testimony and is discretionary – witnesses are not required for acceptance.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, two types of letters are sent between judges: the sijill (a record of a final judgment) and the kitab hukmi (a transmission of testimony). Understanding the difference is crucial because a sijill must be executed by the receiving judge without review, while a kitab hukmi allows him to use his own ijtihad. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines both types and discusses a key dispute: are witnesses required to verify the letter before acting upon it?

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of judicial letters
The passage defines 'sijill' as a judgment record and 'kitab hukmi' as a testimony transmission. Note the Arabic terms and their functional definitions.
Sijill is binding; kitab hukmi is not.
2
Understand the binding effect
The sijill contains a final judgment and is binding on the receiving judge. The kitab hukmi merely transmits testimony; the receiving judge exercises his own ijtihad.
The receiving judge may contradict the sender's opinion in a kitab hukmi.
3
Analyze the condition of witnesses
The passage presents a dispute: Zahir al-Riwayah requires witnesses to verify the letter; Nawadir does not. Identify the reasoning for each.
Zahir al-Riwayah analogizes to testimony; Nawadir prioritizes practicality.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
According to al-Bazzaziyyah, fatwa is given on the Nawadir position: witnesses are not a condition. The letter may be accepted upon seeing the seal.
This is the operative ruling for fatwa today.
5
Consider exceptions
The passage notes that a letter of aman (safe conduct) is accepted without witnesses because it is not binding. Also, registers of sellers, money changers, and brokers are considered proof.
These exceptions are based on the non-binding nature or practical necessity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

شهادته له كالوالدين والزوجة جاز القضاء به بخلاف ما إذا ترافعوا إليه من غير كتاب اهـ . قوله : (فإن شهدا على خصم حاضر حكم بالشهادة لوجود الحجة وشرط الحكم وهو حضور الخصم، والمراد بالخصم الحاضر من كان The book of Hawala, chapter of the judge's letter to another judge, etc., with testimony and he writes his judgment and it is called a sijill, otherwise he does not judge, and he writes the testimony so that the one written to may judge. Then he said: And if the letter that came to him is from one whose testimony is not acceptable for him, like parents and spouse, it is permissible to judge by it, contrary to if they came to him without a letter, end. He said: (If they testify against a present opponent, he judges by the testimony due to the presence of evidence and the condition of judgment, which is the presence of the opponent. And what is meant by the present opponent is one who is an agent from the defendant's side or a musakhkhar, who is appointed by the judge as an agent for the absent to hear the claim against him. Otherwise, if he meant by the opponent the defendant, there would be no need for the letter to the other judge because the opponent is present before the judge and he has already judged against him. When he judges, he writes his judgment to the judge of the town where the principal is to claim the right from him. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. He said: (And he writes his judgment, and it is called a sijill) so that the case is not forgotten over time and so that the letter reminds him of it; otherwise, there is no need to write the judgment because the opponent has appeared himself or his representative, except if it is possible that he absented himself after the judgment and denied it, then he writes it for him to hand over his right or to execute his judgment. In al-Misbah, al-sijill is the judge's document, plural sijillat. I gave a man an sijal: I wrote a document for him. The judge 'sajjala' (with tashdid): he judged, ruled, and recorded his judgment in the sijill. So the sijill is the proof that contains the judge's judgment, but this is in their custom. In our custom, the sijill is a large book in which the affairs of people and what the judge judges and what is written against him are recorded. He said: (And otherwise he does not judge) i.e., if the opponent is not present, he does not judge, because judging an absent person is not permissible as is known. But if a judge judged on an absent person and then the case is transferred to another, he executes it, unlike the kitab hukmi, where it is not executed contrary to his madhhab because the first judge considers it a judgment so it is binding, while the second is an initial judgment so it is not permissible for him. Thus the commentator mentioned, and it indicates that if the judge who judged on the absent person is Hanafi, his judgment is not executed, because he says 'he sees that' and it is beneficial because the meaning of their saying 'judgment on the absent person is executed in the more apparent of the two narrations' is if the judge is Shafi'i. He said: (And he writes the testimony so that the one written to may judge by it, and it is the kitab hukmi) attributed to judgment according to its ultimate outcome; it is essentially a transmission of testimony because the writer did not judge by it but only transmitted it to the recipient to judge by it. Therefore, the recipient judges according to his own opinion even if it contradicts the writer's opinion, unlike the sijill, where he cannot oppose it. As for whether the execution of judgment is conditional or not, they narrated from Zahir al-Riwayah that it is a condition, and from the Nawadir narration that it is not a condition, and by it fatwa is given in al-Bazzaziyyah. On this basis, fatwa is given for its acceptance from a judge of a rural district to a judge of a city or to a rural district. End. Some virtuous people mentioned that in al-Bazzaziyyah it is explicitly stated that the dispute in this issue is built upon the dispute regarding the condition of being a city. He said: (And if he meant by the opponent the defendant, there would be no need, etc.) He said in al-Nahr: And I say in the commentary: The sijill is written only so that the case is not forgotten over time and so that the document reminds him of it; otherwise, there is no need to write it due to the presence of the opponent himself or his representative, except if it is possible that he absented himself after the judgment and denied the judgment, then he writes it for him to hand over his right or to execute his judgment. End. And this, as you see, is explicit in that the judgment... Then it reaches) the recipient looks at its seal and does not accept it without an opponent and witnesses) because it is for judgment, so he does not accept it except in the presence of the opponent like testimony, and the witnesses must be Muslims even if the letter is from a dhimmi to a dhimmi because they testify about the action of a Muslim. They are only needed if the opponent denies that it is the judge's letter; if he admits it, there is no need for them, unlike the letter of aman to the people of war, which is acted upon without evidence because it is not binding. Its meaning is if the letter comes from their king requesting aman, as in al-Inayah. We have written in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that handwriting is not acted upon except in the case of the letter of aman, and in the register of the seller, the money changer, and the broker it is proof. What is meant by not accepting it without an opponent is not reading it, not merely accepting it because no ruling is attached to it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. Abu Yusuf permitted accepting it without evidence but it is not acted upon. What is meant by opponent is either the defendant or his agent. And if the opponent were meant to be the defendant, the letter to the other judge would have the benefits already known. As for judgment on the musakhkhar, it is transmitted from al-Dhakhirah that there are two narrations. He said: The reliance is that if the judge knows that he is musakhkhar, he executes his judgment; otherwise he executes.
Source pages p. 5

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on the chapter from Kanz al-Daqa'iq about judges writing to other judges. The commentator defines two types of judicial letters: the sijill (record of judgment) and the kitab hukmi (letter transmitting testimony). The sijill contains a final judgment, so when it reaches another judge, it is binding and he must execute it without re-examining the case. The kitab hukmi, however, merely transmits the testimony of witnesses; the receiving judge judges based on his own ijtihad, even if it contradicts the sender's view. This is because the sender did not issue a judgment. The passage also discusses conditions: the opponent must be present or represented (by an agent or musakhkhar) for judgment to proceed. If the opponent is absent, the Hanafi view generally does not allow judgment against an absent person, though it is permitted in the Shafi'i school. The condition of witnesses to verify the letter is debated: according to Zahir al-Riwayah, witnesses are required; according to Nawadir, they are not, and the latter is relied upon for fatwa. Abu Yusuf allowed accepting the letter without witnesses but said it should not be acted upon. The passage also notes exceptions: a letter of aman (safe conduct) to enemies is accepted without witnesses because it is not binding, and private registers of sellers, money changers, and brokers are considered proof.

Key Terms
سجل (sijill): A document or record containing a judge's final judgment, which is binding on another judge who receives it.
كتاب حكمي (kitāb ḥukmī): A letter from a judge transmitting testimony of witnesses without issuing a final judgment; the receiving judge judges based on his own ijtihad.
خصم حاضر (khaṣm ḥāḍir): A present opponent in court, either the defendant himself, his agent, or a musakhkhar (court-appointed representative for an absent party).
مسخر (musakhkhar): A person appointed by the judge as a representative for an absent defendant to hear the claim and respond on his behalf.
ظاهر الرواية (Zāhir al-Riwāyah): The established transmitted rulings in the six major works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, considered the primary source of Hanafi law.
رواية النوادر (Riwāyat al-Nawādir): Rare or less common transmissions from the Hanafi imams, not part of the six core books, sometimes given preference in fatwa for practical reasons.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the text, what is required for a judge to accept a kitab hukmi?
A) The letter must be accompanied by two Muslim witnesses.
B) The opponent must be present or represented, and the letter's authenticity is confirmed by the seal.
C) The sender must be a Hanafi judge.
D) The letter must be from a judge in the same city.

Key Takeaway
The sijill is a binding judgment record; the kitab hukmi is a discretionary testimony transmission; witnesses are not required for acceptance per the mufta bihi.

The Judge's Letter: Definition and Discretion
Defines judicial letter and states non-obligation of acceptance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a judge's letter (kitab) and a recorded judgment (sijill), and state when acceptance is obligatory or discretionary according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A judge may accept or reject another judge's letter in ijtihadi matters; only a sijill is binding.

In Hanafi judicial practice, written communications between judges are common, but not all carry the same legal weight. This lesson clarifies the nature of a judge's letter, its distinction from a recorded judgment (sijill), and the discretion afforded to the recipient judge. We will also explore a notable exception for the supplementary oath (yamin al-mudafa) based on necessity and prevention of fitna.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition and key terms
Read the opening passage to understand what a judge's letter (kitab al-qadi) is. Note that it is called 'al-kitab al-hukmi' and is essentially a transfer of testimony. Contrast it with a sijill, which is a formally recorded judgment.
The letter is informational; the sijill is authoritative.
2
Extract the ruling from Munyat al-Mufti
Analyze the cited text: a recipient judge is not required to enforce a letter concerning a matter of ijtihad (where there is ikhtilaf). However, if a sijill arrives, it must be enforced because it is a binding judgment.
The ruling is that acceptance of a letter is mubah (permissible) but not wajib; acceptance of a sijill is wajib.
3
Analyze the practical exception from al-Halwani
Review the anecdote about al-Halwani observing that Hanafi judges would respond to Shafi'i judges in the case of the yamin al-mudafa to prevent hardship and fitna. This shows that pragmatic considerations can override the general discretion.
This exception is based on necessity and the strength of the Shafi'i evidence.
4
Synthesize the mufta bihi position
Combine the general rule and the exception: the operative position is that a judge's letter is not binding, but a sijill is. The yamin al-mudafa is a special case where acceptance is recommended to avoid fitna.
This is the fatwa relied upon according to Munyat al-Mufti and al-Halwani's practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره إليه بها وهو الكتاب الحكمي وهو نقل الشهادة في الحقيقة وقرأ عليهم وختم عندهم وينقض حكمه. وفي منية المفتي ورد كتاب قاض إلى قاض آخر في حادثة لا يراه القاضي المكتوب إليه وهي مختلف فيها لا ينفذه وإن ورد فيها سجل نفذه لأن السجل محكوم به دون الكتاب، ولهذا له أن لا يقبل الكتاب دون السجل ا هـ . فقد أفاد عدم وجوب قبول الكتاب على المكتوب إليه . وفي كتاب المحاضر والسجلات من الظهيرية قال القاضي الإمام ثقة الدين محمد بن علي الحلواني صحبت كثيراً من القضاة الكبار فما رأيتهم أجابوا إلى شيء من الحوادث المجتهد فيها في الكتابة إلى القاضي الشافعي إلا في اليمين المضافة، فإن دلائل أصحاب الحديث في ذلك واضحة وبراهينهم فيها لائحة والشبان يتجاسرون إلى هذه اليمين ثم يحتاجون إلى التزوج فيضطرون إلى ذلك، فلو لم يجبهم القاضي إلى ذلك ربما يقعون في الفتنة ا هـ
Book of Hawalah. Chapter of the Judge's Letter to Another Judge etc. To him through it, and it is the written judgment, and it is transferring testimony in reality, and he read it to them and sealed it in their presence, and it nullifies his ruling. And in Munyat al-Mufti: A letter of a judge to another judge regarding an incident that the recipient judge does not see (as valid) and it is a matter of difference, he does not enforce it; if a record (sijill) comes regarding it, he enforces it because the record is a judgment, unlike the letter. Therefore, he may not accept the letter without the record. So it indicates that accepting the letter is not obligatory upon the recipient judge. And in Kitab al-Muhadir wa al-Sijillat from al-Zahiriyya: The judge-imam Thiqat al-Din Muhammad ibn Ali al-Halwani said, 'I accompanied many senior judges, and I did not see them respond to any of the ijtihad-based cases in writing to the Shafi'i judge except in the case of the supplementary oath (al-yamin al-mudafa). For the proofs of the Hadith scholars on this are clear and their evidences are manifest, and young men are audacious regarding this oath, then they need to marry and are forced to do so. If the judge did not respond to them, perhaps they would fall into fitna.'
Source pages p. 6

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter by defining the judge's letter (kitab al-qadi) as a 'hikmi' letter that essentially transfers testimony. The author then cites Munyat al-Mufti to state that a judge's letter regarding a matter of ijtihad (where there is ikhtilaf) is not to be enforced by the recipient judge unless it is a sijill (a recorded judgment). The reason is that the sijill is a binding judgment, whereas the letter is merely a communication. Therefore, the recipient has the discretion to reject it. The author adds a practical anecdote from al-Halwani, who observed that Hanafi judges would accommodate Shafi'i judges in the case of the 'yamin al-mudafa' (a specific oath) to prevent hardship and fitna. This shows that while the general rule is non-obligation, pragmatic exceptions exist.

Key Terms
كتاب القاضي (kitab al-qadi): A written communication from one judge to another, often containing testimony or a judgment.
الكتاب الحكمي (al-kitab al-hukmi): The formal judicial letter that serves as a transfer of testimony.
السجل (al-sijill): A recorded judgment that has been formally documented and is binding.
اليمين المضافة (al-yamin al-mudafa): The supplementary oath; a specific oath in marriage-related cases where there is disagreement between schools.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When it is a letter (kitab) from a judge.
B) When it is a recorded judgment (sijill).
C) When it is about a matter of ijtihad.
D) When the letter is sealed.

Key Takeaway
A judge's letter is not binding in ijtihadi matters; only a sijill is obligatory to accept, except for the yamin al-mudafa where acceptance is recommended to prevent fitna.
