When Can a Judge Delegate Authority? The Hanafi Position
Commentary on judicial delegation ruling with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on judicial delegation and explain why a judge's unauthorized appointment results in suspended (mawquf) judgments.

Core Idea
A judge cannot delegate unless authorized; unauthorized delegation yields suspended judgments needing ratification.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iʼq explains a key limit on a judge's power: he cannot appoint a deputy judge without explicit permission from the ruler. The ruling is based on the principle that the judge's authority is a delegated trust, akin to an agency. Without authorization, the deputy's judgments are suspended, awaiting ratification. Let's unpack the reasoning and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling
Locate the matn statement: “A judge does not appoint a deputy unless authorization for that is given to him.” This is the foundation.
The matn is the base text; the commentary expands it.
2
Understand the Condition for Delegation
The judge may delegate only if his charter (manshur) explicitly permits it or if a general statement like “whatever you do is valid” is given. Without this, delegation is prohibited.
The condition is analogous to agency law.
3
Analyze the Suspended Status (Mawquf)
If a judge delegates without authorization, the deputy's judgments are not void ab initio but suspended pending ratification by the original judge or ruler. Ratification can make them valid if the judgment itself would be sound.
This mirrors the fiqh concept of ‘aqd al-fuduli.’
4
Distinguish Two Scenarios: With and Without Permission
If the caliph permits delegation, the appointed deputy becomes a judge directly under the caliph—independent of the appointing judge. If not permitted, the deputy's judgments remain suspended.
The source of authority shifts: from the ruler, not the first judge.
5
Trace the Analogy to Agency and Fuduli
The commentary equates judicial delegation to agency (wakala). Just as a wakeel cannot appoint a sub-wakeel without permission, a judge cannot delegate. The fuduli (unauthorized person) in judiciary is analogous to the fuduli in sales.
This is the dalil (proof) by qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قول المصنف : (ولا يستخلف قاض إلا أن يفوض إليه قال الرملي وفي التتارخانية نقلاً. شرح الطحاوي: وليس للقاضي أن يولي القضاء غيره إلا إذا كان مكتوباً في منشوره ذلك أو قيل له ما صنعت من شيء فهو جائز فإن ولى غيره من غير هذا يكون قضاؤه موقوفاً على إجازة الأوّل (م) ولو أن الخليفة لم يأذن له في الاستخلاف فأمر رجلاً فحكم بين اثنين لم يجز حكمه، ثم إن القاضي لو أجاز ذلك الحكم ينظر ؛ إن كان بحال يجوز حكمه لو كان قاضياً جاز إمضاء القاضي حكمه، وإن كان بحال لا يجوز حكمه لو كان قاضياً ينظر إن كان مما يختلف فيه الفقهاء كالمحدود في القذف جاز إمضاؤه ذلك، وإن كان عبداً أو صبياً لم يجز، وإن كان الخليفة أذن للقاضي في الاستخلاف فاستخلف غيره جاز فهذا القاضي الثاني يصير قاضياً من جهة الخليفة لا من جهة القاضي الأوّل حتى لو أراد القاضي الأوّل أن يعزل الثاني لم يكن له ذلك إلا إذا قال الخليفة للأوّل تستبدل من شئت اهـ قوله : (فدخل الفضولي في القضاء المصرح به أن القضاء عقد من العقود وأن كل عقد لا مجيز له حال صدوره من الفضولي لا يتوقف فكذا يجب أن يكون قضاء الفضولي إن كان له مجيز حال صدوره يتوقف ومالا فلا كدار الحرب حيث لا سلطان ولا قاضي ولو قضى بعد منعه ينبغي أن يكون موقوفاً، وفتوى من أفتى بعدم الصحة محمولة على عدم النفاذ حتى يجاز، وبعدم صحة إجازته محمول على الإجازة الإجمالية فتأمل والله تعالى الموفق ؛ كذا بخط بعض الفضلاء قوله : (وظاهر كلامهم الخ) قال الرملي : بل هو داخل في قولهم كلما صح التوكيل إذا باشره الفضولي يتوقف وفي قولهم كل عقد صدر وله مجيز حال وقوعه انعقد موقوفاً على إجازته والقضاء عقد من العقود الشرعية يصح التوكيل فيه بشرطه تأمل، وتقدم الكلام في ذلك في بيع الفضولي قوله : (القاضي إذا قضى للأمير
The author's statement: 'A judge does not appoint a deputy unless authorization for that is given to him.' Al-Ramli said, and in al-Tatarkhaniyya quoting from Sharh al-Tahawi: It is not permissible for a judge to appoint another to the judiciary unless it is written in his charter of appointment or it is said to him 'Whatever you do is valid.' If he appoints someone else without this, his judgment is suspended pending the ratification of the first. (M) And if the caliph did not permit him to appoint a deputy, and he commanded a man to judge between two people, his judgment is not valid. Then if the judge ratifies that judgment, it is considered: if the judgment would have been valid had he been a judge, then the judge's ratification makes it valid; if it would not have been valid had he been a judge, then if it is a matter about which jurists differ, like the one who has been flogged for slander, his ratification makes it valid; but if he is a slave or a minor, it is not valid. And if the caliph permitted the judge to appoint a deputy, and he appointed someone else, then that second judge becomes a judge by virtue of the caliph, not by virtue of the first judge, so that if the first judge wanted to dismiss the second, he does not have that right unless the caliph said to the first 'You may replace whomever you wish.' (End) His statement: 'So the unauthorized person (fuduli) enters into the judiciary.' It is explicitly stated that judiciary is a contract among contracts, and that any contract that has no ratifier at the time of its issuance from the unauthorized person does not become suspended. Thus, the judiciary of an unauthorized person: if it has a ratifier at the time of issuance, it becomes suspended; otherwise, no, like the land of war where there is no sultan or judge. And if he judges after being prevented, it should be suspended. The fatwa of whoever gave a fatwa of unsoundness is interpreted as unsoundness of enforcement until ratification, and unsoundness of ratification is interpreted as general ratification. So reflect, and Allah is the Grantor of success. Thus in the handwriting of some scholars. His statement: 'And the apparent meaning of their statement, etc.' Al-Ramli said: Rather, it is included in their statement 'Whenever agency is valid, if performed by an unauthorized person, it is suspended' and in their statement 'Every contract that occurs and has a ratifier at the time of its occurrence is concluded suspended on his ratification.' Judiciary is a contract among the legal contracts; agency in it is valid with its condition. Reflect. And the discussion on that has preceded in the section on sale by an unauthorized person. His statement: 'The judge if he judges for the ruler'
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the delegation of judicial authority (istikhalf). The matn states that a judge cannot appoint a deputy judge unless he has been specifically authorized to do so by the ruler. The commentator, al-Ramli, elaborates by citing Sharh al-Tahawi and al-Tatarkhaniyya. The key ruling is that without explicit permission in the judge's charter (manshur) or a general authorization ('whatever you do is valid'), any appointment of a deputy by the judge is not immediately effective. Instead, the deputy's judgments are suspended (mawquf) pending ratification by the original judge or the ruler. The passage distinguishes two scenarios: if the caliph (supreme ruler) did not permit delegation, and the judge appoints someone, the appointee's judgments are void unless later ratified by the judge. However, if the caliph had permitted delegation, the appointee becomes a judge directly under the caliph, not under the appointing judge, and thus the appointing judge cannot dismiss him unless the caliph gives that power. The commentary then introduces the concept of the fuduli (unauthorized person) in the judiciary. It explains that if a non-judge issues a judgment without any authority, it can be suspended if there is a ratifier at the time of issuance. Otherwise, it is void. The example given is a land of war where no judge exists; judgments there are not suspended because there is no possible ratifier. The passage clarifies that the ratification of a fuduli's judgment is analogous to ratification in sales by an unauthorized person (bai' al-fuduli). The discussion ends by indicating that a judge's judgment on behalf of the ruler is treated similarly.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhalf): The act of a judge appointing a deputy to act as judge in his place.
فُضولي (fuduli): An unauthorized person who performs a legal act (like judging) without having the authority to do so.
مجيز (mujiz): The person who has the authority to ratify a suspended legal act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in the passage, what is the status of a judgment made by a deputy appointed by a judge without authorization from the ruler?
A) It is batil (void) with no possibility of becoming valid.
B) It is suspended (mawquf) and becomes valid if ratified by the original judge.
C) It is valid because the judge has inherent authority to delegate.
D) It is only valid if the deputy is a qualified judge himself.

Key Takeaway
A judge cannot delegate judicial authority without the ruler's explicit permission; otherwise, the deputy's judgments are suspended and require ratification.

The Judge's Reliance on the Deputy's Statement and Mutual Witnesses
Ruling on deputy's statement in divorce case and mutual witness acceptance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rule that a judge cannot base a severe divorce ruling on a deputy's statement alone, and identify when witness testimony is transferable between judge and deputy.

Core Idea
A judge cannot rely on a deputy's word for a severe prohibition; the deputy's authority is subordinate.

In Islamic judicial procedure, the relationship between a judge and his deputy involves clear limits. A deputy can gather information and hear cases, but his statements do not carry the same weight as the judge's. This lesson examines a specific scenario from al-Bazaziyya where a deputy reports a third divorce, and explores the rules governing mutual acceptance of witnesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Two khul' divorces occurred before the judge. The deputy then claims a third occurred before him, but the husband denies it.
Khul' is a divorce initiated by the wife with compensation.
2
State the Ruling on Deputy's Statement
The judge cannot rule based on the deputy's statement alone for the severe prohibition (third divorce). The text says: 'لا يقضي القاضي بالحرمة الغليظة بكلام النائب'.
The deputy's report lacks judicial authority.
3
State the Ruling on Mutual Witnesses
The deputy can judge using witnesses who testified before the original judge, and vice versa. This implies mutual acceptance of witness testimony between the two.
This facilitates continuity of proceedings.
4
Understand the Deputy's Limited Role
If the judge orders the deputy only to hear and write (not adjudicate), the deputy must comply. The judge cannot then base a ruling on the deputy's report of witnesses, because the deputy is not a judge.
The principle: a non-judge's report is not sufficient for a judgment.
5
Derive the Underlying Principle
Judicial authority is personal; second-hand reports from a subordinate do not substitute for direct evidence or the judge's own cognizance.
This ensures the integrity of judicial decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البزازية جرى الخلع بين الزوجين عند القاضي مرتين فقال نائبه كان قد جرى عندي مرة أخرى والزوج ينكر فقال القاضي الإمام : لا يقضي القاضي بالحرمة الغليظة بكلام النائب، أما النائب يقضي بكلام القاضي إذا أخبره ا هـ ثم قال في نوع في الإمضاء : والنائب يقضي بما شهدوا عند الأصل، وكذا القاضي يقضي بما شهدوا عند النائب، أمر القاضي الخليفة أن القضية والشهادة ويكتب الإقرار ولا يقطع الحكم يفعل ما أمره يسمع القاضي، وليس له أن يحكم ليس للقاضي أن يحكم بأخبار خليفته بشهادة الشهود عنده لأنه ليس بقاض، وكذا لو أخبره بإقراره رجل إلا أن يشهد هو مع آخر
And in al-Bazaziyya: A divorce (khul') occurred between spouses twice before the judge, then his deputy said, 'It had occurred a third time before me,' while the husband denied it. The judge said (the ruling): The judge does not rule based on the severe prohibition by the deputy's statement. However, the deputy rules by the judge's statement if he informs him. Then he said in the type of enforcement: The deputy judges by what they witnessed before the original, and likewise the judge judges by what they witnessed before the deputy. The judge ordered the deputy to hear the case and testimony and write the acknowledgment but not to issue a final ruling; he does what he is ordered. The judge hears, and it is not for him to rule. It is not for the judge to rule based on the report of his deputy about the testimony of witnesses before him because he is not a judge. Similarly, if a man informs him of his acknowledgment, unless he (the informant) witnesses along with another.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage contains several interconnected rulings from al-Bazaziyya regarding the relationship between the original judge and his deputy. In the divorce scenario, two khul' divorces were pronounced before the judge. The deputy then claimed a third had occurred before him, but the husband denied. The ruling: the original judge cannot issue a ruling of severe prohibition (i.e., irrevocable divorce) based solely on the deputy's word. However, the deputy can rule based on the original judge's statement if the judge informs him. This asymmetry shows the higher authority of the original judge. The passage then repeats a principle: the deputy can judge using witnesses who testified before the original judge, and vice versa. Additionally, if the judge orders the deputy only to hear the case and write down acknowledgments without issuing a final ruling, the deputy must comply: he may only listen, not adjudicate. The judge cannot then base a judgment on the deputy's report of witnesses, because the deputy is not a judge himself. The same applies if a man reports someone's acknowledgment: it is not sufficient unless the reporter himself witnesses along with another.

Key Terms
الخلع (al-khul‘): Divorce initiated by the wife in return for compensation.
الحرمة الغليظة (al-ḥurma al-ghalīẓa): Severe prohibition, here referring to the irrevocable divorce after the third divorce.
النائب (al-nā’ib): Deputy judge; in this context, subordinate to the original judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario from al-Bazaziyya, why does the judge not rule based solely on the deputy's statement that a third divorce occurred?
A) Because the deputy is not a witness.
B) Because the deputy is not a judge and his statement lacks judicial authority.
C) Because the husband denied it.
D) Because the divorce occurred before the judge, not the deputy.

Key Takeaway
A judge cannot rely on a deputy's statement for a severe prohibition due to the subordinate nature of the deputy's authority.

Testimony of Sub-Judges and Fabricated Agents
Ruling on acceptance of testimony of sub-judges and agents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of maskhara al-qadi and fabricated agents, and distinguish it from the testimony of just deputies.

Core Idea
The testimony of sub-judges and fabricated agents is rejected, but just deputies' testimony is accepted — reliability determines admissibility.

This lesson examines a practical issue from the Hanafi judiciary: whose testimony is accepted in court? The passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that informal assistants and false agents are not credible, while formally appointed just deputies retain their testimonial capacity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for ensuring the integrity of judicial proceedings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
Highlight the three types mentioned: maskhara al-qadi (sub-judges), al-wukala' al-mufta'ala (fabricated agents), and nuwwab (deputies).
The text uses 'بخلاف نوابهم' to contrast deputies.
2
Note the ruling for each
The first two are not accepted (la tuqbal); deputies are accepted but only if they are just (ahl al-adl).
Justness is a condition for testimony generally.
3
Understand the reasoning
The rejection is due to lack of formal appointment and potential dishonesty; the acceptance of just deputies is based on their reliability.
The author notes the laxity in Dasht as a cause for concern.
4
Apply the distinction
Consider a scenario: a person presents himself as a deputy but has no formal appointment. His testimony would be rejected like that of a fabricated agent.
Formal appointment is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تناطقت أجوبة أئمتنا بخوارزم أن شهادة مسخرة القاضي وشهادة الوكلاء المفتعلة ببابه لا تقبل بخلاف نوابهم، إلا أهل العدل، وقد رأيت بنواحي خوارزم وبها جماعة ممن فوض إليهم القضاء، وكذا ببعض نواحي دشت لا يصح القضاء بشهادتهم فكيف قضاؤهم؟ وسئلت عن شهادة بعضهم أنه تقبل نقلت : نعم تقبل مع عدلين وكل ذلك من تهاون أمر الدشت بالشرع. وقد رأيت من العجائب أن واحداً من أمرائه الذي يدعي أنه لم يمض مثله ديناً قلد قضاء مدينة إلى شاب جاهل لا يعرف قرآناً ولا خطاً حتى يقضي بأربعة مذاهب فقلت له فيه فقال : أنا أعلم بالمصلحة والله يعلم المفسد من المصلح اه. فالحاصل أن القاضي إذا ولي الخليفة القضاء عمل : الذي قلده الخ) قال الرملي وجهه أن القاضي نائب عن العامة كما في المحيط، وإذا كان كذلك فلم يكن فعله منسوباً إليه ومن قال بأن القاضي نائب عن السلطان فلعل وجهه عنده انحصار الطريق فيه إذ الحكم من الإمام بمنزلة القاضي المولى لا طريق إلى التحكيم فجاز ذلك فتحاً لباب القضاء له، وسيأتي أن الحكم من الإمام يمنزلة القاضي ولم أر من حرر ذلك تأمل قوله : (وفي البزازية كما في السراجية) قال الرملي : فثبت أن في المسألة قولين وجه ما في السراجية أن الخليفة ليس نائباً عنه وإنما هو نائب عن السلطان أو العامة فانقطعت النسبة لكن في الأشباه والنظائر أنهم نواب القاضي في زماننا من كل وجه وعليه ينبغي ترجيح ما في الملتقط لما في قضائه له من التهمة إذ فعل النائب كفعله فصار كأنه قضى لنفسه ولم أر من رجح أحد القولين والله تعالى أعلم. قوله : (أمر القاضي (الخليفة أي خليفة القاضي قوله : (اهـ) أي كلام البزازية قوله : (لكنه ذكر
And the answers of our imams in Khwarazm concurred that the testimony of the subservient (?) deputies and the fabricated agents at his door is not accepted, contrary to their deputies, except the just. And I saw in the regions of Khwarazm a group of those who were delegated to the judiciary, and likewise in some regions of Dasht, judgment is not valid by their testimony, so how can their judgment be valid? I was asked about the testimony of some of them: is it accepted? I reported: Yes, it is accepted with two just witnesses, and all that is due to the laxity of the people of Dasht regarding the Sharia. And I saw of the wonders that one of his princes, who claimed that no one like him had passed in religion, appointed a young ignorant man as judge of a city who did not know the Quran or writing, to the extent that he would judge according to four madhhabs. I said to him about it, and he said: 'I know better about the benefit, and Allah knows the corrupt from the reformer.' So the conclusion is that the judge, when he appoints the deputy to act as judge, (said Ramli: his reasoning is that the judge is a deputy of the public as in al-Muhit. If that is the case, then his action is not attributed to him. And whoever said that the judge is a deputy of the sultan, perhaps his reasoning is that the path is limited to him, for the ruling from the imam is like that of the judge who is appointed; there is no path to arbitration, so it is permitted to open the door of judiciary for him. It will come that the ruling from the imam is like that of the judge. I have not seen anyone who has refined this. Reflect on his statement: 'And in al-Bazaziyya as in al-Sirajiyya.' Ramli said: So it is established that there are two opinions on the matter; the face of what is in al-Sirajiyya is that the deputy is not a deputy of him but rather a deputy of the sultan or the public, so the relation is cut off. However, in al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, they are deputies of the judge in our time in every aspect, and accordingly, preference should be given to what is in al-Multaqat because of the accusation in his judging for himself, for the action of the deputy is like his action, so it becomes as if he judged for himself. And I have not seen anyone who has preferred one of the two opinions, and Allah knows best.) His statement: 'The judge ordered the deputy,' i.e., the deputy of the judge. His statement: 'end' i.e., the words of al-Bazaziyya. His statement: 'but he mentioned'
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a multi-layered discussion combining practical issues, historical observations, and theoretical analysis. It begins with a consensus from Hanafi scholars in Khwarazm that the testimony of 'مسخرة القاضي' (likely sub-judges or assistants) and fabricated agents at the judge's door is not accepted, but the testimony of just deputies is accepted. The author observes that in Khwarazm and Dasht, even those delegated with judicial authority are not trustworthy, and their judgments are invalid. However, when asked about accepting their testimony, the author says it is accepted if accompanied by two just witnesses, indicating a distinction between iqamat al-shahada (providing testimony) and qada (judgment). The author then recounts a story of a prince appointing an ignorant man as judge, criticizing the prince's dismissal of Sharia. The passage then shifts to a theoretical discussion from Ramli: The judge is a deputy of the public (not the sultan), so his action is not attributed to him personally. There are two opinions: one that the deputy is a deputy of the judge, another that he is directly a deputy of the sultan or public. In the later period, as per al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir, they are considered deputies of the judge from all aspects, and the preference is for the ruling that the deputy's action is like the judge's, hence the judge cannot delegate a case involving himself due to accusation. However, no definitive tarjih is given. The passage ends with the beginning of a commentary on the phrase 'أمر القاضي' (the judge ordered), which continues on the next page.

Key Terms
مسخرة القاضي (maskhara al-qaḍī): Sub-judges or assistants employed by the judge; their testimony is not accepted.
الوكلاء المفتعلة (al-wukalā’ al-mufta‘ala): Fabricated agents; those who falsely claim agency.
النائب (al-nā’ib): The formally appointed deputy judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, whose testimony is not accepted?
A) Just deputies
B) Sub-judges (مسخرة القاضي)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Only just and formally appointed deputies may testify; sub-judges and fabricated agents are rejected.

Who Does the Deputy Represent? The Two Views in Hanafi Fiqh
Theoretical debate on the nature of the deputy judge's representation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the two Hanafi views on whether a deputy judge represents the judge or the sultan/public, and identify the preferred view in later fatwa.

Core Idea
The deputy judge's relationship to the appointing judge determines if his actions are like the judge's own — a debate with practical implications.

This lesson delves into a theoretical but consequential debate within the Hanafi school: when a judge appoints a deputy (khalifa), does the deputy act as the judge's representative or as a direct representative of the sultan or the public? The answer affects whether the judge can delegate a case involving himself. We will follow the discussion from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
One view (from Ramli) is that the judge is a deputy of the public, so the deputy's action is not attributed to the judge. Another view (from al-Sirajiyya) is that the deputy is a deputy of the sultan or public, cutting the relation to the judge.
The text references al-Muhit for the first view.
2
Identify the practical consequence
If the deputy represents the judge, then judging a case involving the judge would be like judging for oneself (tuhma/accusation). Hence the judge cannot delegate such a case.
This is the key implication.
3
Note the preferred view
Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir states that in our time, deputies are deputies of the judge from all aspects. Therefore, preference is given to the ruling of al-Multaqat that the judge cannot delegate a case involving himself.
No definitive tarjih is given, but this is the relied-upon view.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Delegation of Judge Before Reaching Jurisdiction
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation before arrival

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a judge's delegation before arrival and apply the author's tarjih based on current practice.

Core Idea
A judge can delegate before reaching his post only if authorized by the sultan or by established practice — a dispute settled by necessity.

This lesson explores a nuanced issue in Hanafi judicial procedure: may a judge appoint a deputy before physically arriving at the city where he is to judge? The passage presents a disagreement between al-Sadr al-Shahid and the later Hanafi commentator in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq. By analyzing the proofs and the role of current practice, you will understand how later Hanafi jurists reconciled conflicting principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Question
Determine the exact act under dispute: a judge appointing a deputy (istikhilaf) before reaching his designated place of judgment.
Focus on the timing of delegation, not the validity of the appointment itself.
2
State al-Sadr al-Shahid's Position
Al-Sadr al-Shahid holds that a judge only gains authority upon arrival at his jurisdiction; before that, he is like a subject and cannot delegate.
His proof: the first judge is not removed until the new judge arrives, showing that physical presence is essential.
3
State the Author's Position
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq argues that delegation before arrival is valid, especially for the chief judge (qadi al-qudat) or when it is the established practice.
He cites analogy to agency and the necessity of not delaying justice.
4
Analyze the Qiyas on Agency
The author draws an analogy to an agent who can act before receiving the principal's physical property? Actually, he argues that just as an agent's authorization is effective immediately, so is the judge's power to delegate.
The illa (effective cause) is that both are appointed by a superior authority and their capacity is not tied to physical presence at a specific place.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih and Current Practice
The author gives weight to the fact that in his time (and the Ottoman practice), judges regularly sent deputies ahead, and this was accepted by the sultan. This custom (al-waqi' al-an) becomes a basis for validity.
Consider how later Hanafi jurists prioritize practical necessity and implicit authorization over strict textual reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره بقوله وإن ولاه سماع الدعوى والشهادة فقط لا يعمل بقوله فلا تناقض كما لا يخفى. وفائدة هذا الاستخلاف أن ينظر الخليفة هل للمدعي شهود أو يكذب فلعل له شهوداً إلا أنهم غير عدول، وقد لا تتفق ألفاظهم فيفوّض القاضي النظر إلى الخليفة كذا في الخانية. وقد سئلت عن صحة تولية القاضي ابنه قاضياً حيث كان مأذوناً له بالاستخلاف فأجبت بنعم والله أعلم. أطلق في الاستخلاف فشمل ما إذا كان مذهب الخليفة موافقاً لمذهب القاضي أو مخالفاً. وفي البزازية : ولو فوض إلى غيره ليقضي على وفق مذهبه نفذ إجماعاً ا هـ. وظاهر إطلاقهم أن المأذون له بالاستخلاف صريحاً أو دلالة يملكه قبل الوصول إلى محل قضائه كما يملكه بعده، وقد جرت عادتهم إذا ولوا ببلد السلطان قضاء بلدة بعيدة بإرسال خليفة يقوم مقامهم إلى حضورهم، وقد سئلت عنها في سنة تسع وتسعين وتسعمائة فأجبت بذلك والله الموفق. ثم رأيت الأجل الصدر الشهيد في شرح أدب القضاء للخصاف قال في الباب السادس عشر : القاضي إنما يصير قاضياً إذا بلغ الموضع الذي قلد فيه القضاء، ألا ترى أن الأول لا ينعزل البلد الذي قلد فيه القضاء فكان هو في ذلك المكان بمنزلة واحد من الرعايا ا هـ. وهو يفيد أن القاضي لا يملك الاستخلاف قبل وصوله إلى محل عمله لكنه ذكر في الباب السادس أنه ينبغي للقاضي أن يقدم نائبه قبل وصوله حتى يتعرف عن أحوال الناس إلا أن يقال : إن قاضي القضاة مأذون بالاستخلاف قبل الوصول من السلطان فلا كلام، وهذا هو الواقع الآن. وقيد باستخلافه قاضياً لأن له التوكيل والإيصاء بلا إذن السلطان، وأورد هذا إشكالاً على منعه من تقليد القضاء فإن التعليل المذكور يجري فيها. وأجاب عنه في العناية بأن المقلد يفعل ما لا يفعله الوكيل والوصي فيكون توقع الفساد في القضاء أكثر ا هـ. لم يبلغ اهـ. هو في الباب السادس الخ قال في النهر : ومقتضى الأوّل أنه لا يستخلف والثاني أنه يستخلف فيحمل على إرسال النائب بإذن الخليفة أو أن ذلك معروف بينهم .اهـ. وفي حاشية أبي السعود وأقول : جواز إرساله لتعرف أحوال الناس لا يستفاد منه جواز حكمه قبل وصول المرسل. ثم رأيت بخط السيد الحموي عن بعض الفضلاء ما نصه : ليس المراد محض الإرسال إنما المراد الإرسال على أن يحكم وهو ممنوع الخ. وحينئذ فلا يعوّل على ما أفتى به صاحب البحر من جواز استخلافه قبل وصوله إلى محل قضائه اهـ. ما في الحاشية. وأقول : لا يخفى أن الكلام في صحة الاستخلاف، ولا شك أن قوله «نائبه» يفيد ذلك وأما إن له أن يحكم أو لا فبحث آخر لا ذكر له في كلام صاحب البحر لا سيما وقد انضم إليه أنه الواقع الآن، وقد ذكر أوائل كتاب القضاء وإذا عزله السلطان لا ينعزل ما لم يصل إليه الخبر كالوكيل، وعن الثاني ما لم يأت قاض آخر صيانة للمسلمين من تعطيل قضاياهم اهـ. فما مشى عليه الصدر الشهيد أولاً . على هذه الرواية وحيث كانت العلة ما ذكر فلا مانع من وصول نائبه كوصوله فيفيد أن لنائبه الحكم تأمل . مبني . أن يقال
Book of Hawala, Chapter of the Judge's Letter to Another Judge etc. By his saying: 'If he authorized him to hear the claim and testimony only, his statement is not acted upon,' so there is no contradiction as is not hidden. The benefit of this delegation is that the deputy considers whether the plaintiff has witnesses or is lying, for perhaps he has witnesses but they are not upright, and their words may not agree, so the judge entrusts the consideration to the deputy, as in al-Khaniyya. I was asked about the validity of a judge appointing his son as a judge when he was permitted to deputize, so I answered yes, and Allah knows best. He gave an absolute ruling in delegation, so it includes whether the deputy's school agrees with the judge's or differs. In al-Bazzaziyya: If he delegated to another to judge according to his own school, it is executed by consensus, end. The apparent meaning of their absolute statements is that the one permitted to deputize explicitly or implicitly owns it before arriving at his place of judging just as he owns it after. Their practice has been that when they are appointed by the sultan to a distant city, they send a deputy to act in their place until they arrive. I was asked about this in the year 999 [AH] and answered accordingly, and Allah grants success. Then I saw the venerable al-Sadr al-Shahid in his commentary on Adab al-Qada' of al-Khassaf, in the sixteenth chapter: 'A judge only becomes a judge when he reaches the place he is appointed to judge; do you not see that the first [judge] is not removed from the city he was appointed to judge? So he is like one of the subjects in that place.' End. This indicates that the judge does not own delegation before reaching his place of work. But he mentioned in the sixth chapter that a judge should send his deputy before his arrival to learn about the people's conditions. Unless it is said: The chief judge (qadi al-qudat) is permitted to deputize before arrival by the sultan, so no discussion; this is the reality now. He restricted it to his appointing a judge because he has the right of agency and bequest without the sultan's permission. This raises an issue regarding the prohibition of appointing a judge [by delegation?], because the mentioned rationale applies there. He answered in al-'Inayah: The appointee (muqallid) does what the agent and executor do not, so the expectation of corruption in judging is greater. End. He did not reach... End. It is in the sixth chapter, etc. In al-Nahr: The requirement of the first is that he does not deputize, and the second that he does; so it is interpreted as sending the deputy with the permission of the caliph, or that it is known among them. End. In the gloss of Abu al-Sa'ud: I say: Permission to send him to learn about people's conditions does not imply permission for him to judge before the sender arrives. Then I saw in the handwriting of al-Sayyid al-Hamawi from some scholars: The intended meaning is not mere sending; rather, the intended meaning is sending him to judge, which is prohibited, etc. Therefore, what the author of al-Bahr gave as a fatwa about the permissibility of his deputizing before arriving at his place of judging is not relied upon. End of what is in the gloss. I say: It is not hidden that the discussion is about the validity of deputizing. There is no doubt that his words 'his deputy' indicate that. As for whether he has the right to judge or not, that is another discussion not mentioned in the words of the author of al-Bahr, especially since it is coupled with the fact that it is the current practice. He mentioned in the beginning of the Book of Judging: 'When the sultan removes him, he is not removed until the news reaches him, like an agent; and according to the second view, until another judge comes, to safeguard Muslims from the suspension of their cases.' End. So what al-Sadr al-Shahid proceeded with first... according to this narration; and since the rationale is what was mentioned, there is no obstacle to the arrival of his deputy like his own arrival, so it indicates that his deputy has the right to judge. Consider. Based on... that is to be said.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage examines whether a judge may appoint a deputy (خليفة) before physically reaching the city where he is to judge. The base text says that if a judge authorizes someone only to hear claims and testimony, his statement is not binding. The commentary explains the benefit of such delegation: the deputy checks if the plaintiff actually has witnesses. The author then discusses a fatwa he gave about a judge appointing his son as deputy, stating it is valid if the judge is permitted to deputize. He notes that delegation is valid even if the deputy's school differs from the judge's, as per consensus in al-Bazzaziyya. The issue arises: can a judge appoint a deputy before arriving at his jurisdiction? The apparent meaning of earlier texts is that he can, and current practice supports it. However, al-Sadr al-Shahid in his commentary on Adab al-Qada' argues that a judge only becomes a judge when he reaches his place, so he cannot delegate before arrival. Yet al-Sadr also says a judge should send a deputy ahead to learn about the people. The author reconciles: perhaps the chief judge (qadi al-qudat) is exempted because sultan authorizes him. The discussion involves differences among Hanafi scholars. Ultimately, the author defends his earlier fatwa by noting that the current practice (الواقع الآن) supports delegation before arrival, and the rationale of necessity applies. He also notes that a judge being removed only upon hearing of removal is analogous to agency. The passage concludes with a note to consider (تأمل) indicating some ambiguity.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhilaf): The act of a judge appointing a deputy (خليفة) to act in his place.
خليفة (khalifah): A deputy judge appointed by the original judge to hear cases and render judgments.
قاضي القضاة (qadi al-qudat): The chief judge, often appointed by the sultan with broader authority, including the power to delegate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) As soon as he is appointed by the sultan
B) When he reaches the place he is appointed to judge
C) After he sends a deputy ahead
D) Only after he hears his first case

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a judge's delegation before arriving at his post is a contested issue; the later relied-upon position considers it valid when supported by custom or sultanic permission.

Enforcement of a Judge's Ruling in Hanafi Fiqh
Ruling with proof from Ijma and conditions from Hanafi sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a second judge must enforce a first judge's ruling according to the Hanafi school, and explain the requirement of the first judge's knowledge of ijtihad.

Core Idea
A judge must enforce another's ruling only if it rests on valid ijtihad and does not contradict primary sources; the first judge must have known the matter was subject to ijtihad.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on judicial enforcement. When a judge's verdict is presented to another judge, is it automatically binding? The classical texts clarify that enforcement is obligatory unless the ruling contradicts clear divine sources. However, a key condition emerges: the first judge must have been aware that the case was a matter of legitimate scholarly disagreement (ijtihad). This prevents unwitting rulings from being perpetuated. We will unpack the textual proof, the conditions, and the intra-madhhab refinement that settles the operative position.

Guided Path
1
State the basic ruling
Identify the core rule: a judge's ruling must be enforced by another judge unless it contradicts the Quran, well-known Sunnah, or consensus. This is wajib by default.
The ruling is based on ijma (consensus) that judicial decisions are binding.
2
Recognize the absolute application
Note that the ruling applies whether the second judge agrees or disagrees. The text uses an indefinite noun in a conditional context, but the sharh clarifies that it is general.
The author rebuts a commentator who claimed it only applies when the second judge agrees.
3
Identify the condition of knowledge
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, the first judge must have known that the specific case was a matter of ijtihad. Mere general knowledge that the issue is ijtihadi is insufficient.
This condition is supported by Shams al-A'immah and Ibn al-Kamal.
4
Understand the linguistic nuance
The text discusses whether the phrase 'ruling of a judge' is general. The sharh explains that the definite context of a conditional does not automatically give generality; it depends on the type of conditional (oath vs. non-oath).
This linguistic analysis ensures the ruling's scope is correctly derived.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider a judge who rules on selling an umm walad without knowing she is an umm walad. Even if he knows the general issue is ijtihadi, his ruling is not enforceable because he lacked specific knowledge.
This example from Munyat al-Mufti illustrates the practical impact of the condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإذا رفع إليه حكم قاض أمضاه إن لم يخالف الكتاب والسنة المشهورة والإجماع) لترجح الاجتهاد الأول بالقضاء فلا ينقضه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان موافقاً لرأيه أو مخالفاً لكون لفظ الحكم نكرة في سياق الشرط فتعم، فليس في كلامه ما. أنه يوهم إنما يمضيه إذا كان موافقاً لرأيه كما زعم الشارح وفي الجامع الصغير وما اختلف فيه الفقهاء فقضى به القاضي ثم جاء قاض آخر يرى غير ذلك أمضاه . وفي المعراج وإنما ذكر لفظ الجامع بهذا اللفظ المذكور لأنه فيه فائدتين إحداهما أنه قيد بالفقهاء لأن القاضي إذا كان غير عالم بموضع الاجتهاد فاتفق قضاؤه في موضع الاجتهاد فعلى قول عامة المشايخ لا يجب على الثاني تنفيذه. كذا ذكره في فصول الأستروشني محالاً إلى المحيط والذخيرة فقال : لو قضى في فصل مجتهد فيه وهو لا يعلم بذلك، قيل ينفذ قضاؤه وعامتهم لا ينفذ وإنما ينفذ إذا علم بكونه مجتهداً فيه. قال شمس الأئمة : هذا هو ظاهر المذهب. والثاني أنه قيده بقوله «يرى غير ذلك» وفي رواية القدوري لم يتعرض لذلك فيحتمل أن قوله أمضاه» فيما إذا كان موافقاً وفي الخلاصة: إن هذا الشرط يعني كونه عالماً بالاختلاف وإن كان ظاهر المذهب لكن يفتي بخلافه، والتحقيق المعتمد أن علمه بكون ما حكم فيه مجتهداً فيه شرط، وأما علمه بكون المسألة اجتهادية فلا، ويدل عليه ما في الفتاوى الصغرى. وشمل قوله «حكم قاض» ما إذا كان الحكم موافقاً لرأيه أو مخالفاً وما إذا كان القاضي باقياً على قضائه أو مات أو عزل كما الجمعة أفسد صلاته ثم افتتح بهم قوله : (أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان موافقاً لرأيه) أي لرأي القاضي المرفوع إليه حكم القاضي الأول قوله : (لكون لفظ الحكم نكرة في سياق الشرط فتعم) فيه نظر فإن ذلك إذا كان الشرط يميناً مثبتاً مثل إن كلمت رجلاً فكذا فإن المعنى لا أكلم رجلاً فتكون النكرة في سياق النفي معنى فتعم بخلاف قولك اليمين المنفي مثل إن لم أكلم رجلاً فلا : تعم لأنه في معنى لأكلمن رجلاً فهي نكرة في سياق الإثبات، وبخلاف الشرط الواقع غير يمين مثل إن جاءك رجل فأكرمه فإنه أيضاً غير نص في العموم لأنه في سياق الإثبات، وما في المتن من هذا القبيل كما لا يخفى قوله : (والتحقيق المعتمد أن علمه الخ قال ابن الكمال وههنا شرط آخر وهو أن يكون القاضي عالماً بأن ما حكم فيه مجتهد فيه ولا يكفي فيه علمه بأن المسألة مجتهد فيها كما إذا قضى ببيع أم الولد غير عالم بأنها أم ولد فإنه لا يجوز، وإن كان عالماً بأن مسألة أم الولد اجتهادية؛ ذكره في منية المفتى اهـ قوله : ( وشمل قوله حكم قاض ما إذا كان الحكم موافقاً لرأيه) أي لرأي القاضي الأول
His statement: (And when the ruling of a judge is presented to him [another judge], he enforces it as long as it does not contradict the Book, the well-known Sunnah, and consensus). Because the first ijtihad is strengthened by the judgment, so it is not overturned. He made it absolute, so it includes whether it agrees with his own opinion or opposes it, because the word 'ruling' is an indefinite noun in the context of a condition, thus it is general. There is nothing in his speech that suggests he only enforces it if it agrees with his opinion, as the commentator claimed. In al-Jami' al-Saghir: 'Whatever the jurists differ on, and a judge ruled on it, then another judge who holds a different view comes, he enforces it.' In al-Mi'raj: He only mentioned the wording of al-Jami' because it contains two benefits. First, it is restricted to jurists, because if the judge is unaware of the point of ijtihad and happens to rule in a matter of ijtihad, then according to the general opinion of the scholars, the second judge is not obligated to enforce it. Thus it is mentioned in Fusul al-Usrushni, referencing al-Muhit and al-Dhakhirah: 'If he ruled in a matter of ijtihad while not knowing it, it is said his ruling is enforced, but the majority say it is not enforced unless he knows it is a matter of ijtihad.' Shams al-A'immah said: 'This is the apparent madhhab.' Second, he restricted it by his statement 'he holds a different view'. In the narration of al-Quduri, he did not address that, so it is possible that his statement 'he enforces' is only when it agrees. In al-Khulasah: 'This condition, meaning his knowledge of the difference, is the apparent madhhab, but fatwa is given contrary to it.' The verified relied-upon position is that his knowledge that what he ruled on is a matter of ijtihad is a condition. As for his knowledge that the issue is ijtihadi, it is not. This is supported by what is in al-Fatawa al-Sughra. His statement 'ruling of a judge' includes whether the ruling agrees with his opinion or opposes it, and whether the judge remains on his ruling, dies, or is removed. His statement: (He made it absolute, so it includes when it agrees with his opinion) meaning the opinion of the judge to whom the ruling of the first judge is presented. His statement: (because the word 'ruling' is an indefinite noun in the context of a condition, so it is general) warrants consideration, because that is when the condition is a positive oath, like 'If I speak to a man, [then such]', the meaning is 'I will not speak to a man', so the indefinite in the context of negation gives generality. Unlike a negative oath like 'If I do not speak to a man, then not', it is not general because it is in the meaning of 'I will speak to a man', so it is an indefinite in the context of affirmation. And unlike a condition that is not an oath, like 'If a man comes to you, honor him', it is also not explicit in generality because it is in the context of affirmation. And what is in the text is of this category, as is not hidden. His statement: (And the verified relied-upon position is that his knowledge...) Ibn al-Kamal said: There is another condition here: that the judge knows that what he ruled on is a matter of ijtihad, and it is not sufficient for him to know that the issue is ijtihadi, as when he rules on selling an umm walad without knowing she is an umm walad, it is not valid, even if he knows that the issue of umm walad is ijtihadi. This is mentioned in Munyat al-Mufti. His statement: (And his statement 'ruling of a judge' includes when the ruling agrees with his opinion) meaning the opinion of the first judge.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the enforcement of a judge's decision by a subsequent judge. The main ruling is that if a judge's ruling is presented to another judge, the latter must enforce it, provided it does not contradict the Quran, the widely transmitted Sunnah, or consensus (ijma). This is because the first judge's ijtihad is considered valid and his judgment renders it binding; it cannot be overturned simply because the second judge holds a different opinion. The author clarifies that the ruling is absolute: it applies whether the second judge agrees or disagrees with the first judge's opinion, although there is a linguistic discussion about the generality of the phrase. The commentator then discusses conditions: the first judge must have been a scholar aware that the matter was a point of ijtihad. If he ruled in a matter of ijtihad without knowing it was an area of disagreement, the majority of scholars (including Shams al-A'immah) say the second judge is not obligated to enforce it. The relied-upon position (al-mu'tamad) is that the first judge's knowledge that the specific case is a matter of ijtihad is a condition; mere knowledge that the general legal issue is subject to ijtihad is insufficient. For example, if a judge sold an umm walad (a slave who has borne her master's child) without knowing she was an umm walad, the ruling is not valid. The passage also notes that the ruling applies even if the first judge has died or been removed from office.

Key Terms
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihād): A matter that admits of differing legal interpretations among qualified scholars.
نقض الحكم (naqḍ al-ḥukm): Overturning a previous judge's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position in the text, which of the following is required for a second judge to enforce a first judge's ruling?
A) The second judge must agree with the first judge's opinion.
B) The first judge must have known that the specific case was a matter of ijtihad.
C) The first judge must be alive and in office.
D) The ruling must be from a judge of the same madhhab.

Key Takeaway
A subsequent judge must enforce a prior judge's ruling only if it does not contradict primary sources and the first judge was specifically aware that the case was a matter of ijtihad.

Enforcing a Previous Judge's Ruling: The Hanafi Position
Explains the ruling of the second judge enforcing the first judge's judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a second Hanafi judge must enforce the first judge's ruling and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding the first judge's knowledge of ijtihad.

Core Idea
A second judge enforces the first judge's ruling unless the first judge was unaware of the ijtihad.

This lesson examines a key principle in Hanafi judicial procedure: the obligation of a second judge to enforce a prior judge's ruling. The commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies that enforcement is mandatory unless the ruling contradicts definitive texts. However, a significant nuance arises when the first judge was ignorant of the ijtihad nature of the case, leading to a difference within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
The base text states that a second judge must enforce the first judge's ruling unless it contradicts the Quran, well-known sunna, or consensus. Note that the rule is absolute—it applies whether the second judge agrees or disagrees with the ruling.
The phrase 'a ruling of a judge' in a conditional context is interpreted as general.
2
Examine the Commentator's Expansion
The commentator rebuts a claim that the rule only applies when the ruling aligns with the second judge's opinion. He cites al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Mi'raj to support the generality, and notes two benefits of the wording used.
The first benefit: the restriction to 'jurists' indicates that the judge must be aware of ijtihad issues.
3
Analyze the Intra-Madhhab Dispute
If the first judge ruled on a matter of ijtihad without knowing it was such, the majority of Hanafi mashayikh say the second judge need not enforce it. Shams al-A'imma confirms this as the apparent madhhab, though some later texts give a fatwa contrary.
This is a key condition that modifies the general rule.
4
Critique the Grammatical Argument
The passage ends with a critique: the claim that an indefinite in a conditional clause gives generality is challenged. The commentator distinguishes between oaths and non-oath conditions, showing that the text's wording does not automatically imply generality.
This demonstrates the usuli depth of the Hanafi methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وإذا رفع إليه حكم قاض أمضاه إن لم يخالف الكتاب والسنة المشهورة والإجماع) لترجح الاجتهاد الأول بالقضاء فلا ينقضه. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان موافقاً لرأيه أو مخالفاً لكون لفظ الحكم نكرة في سياق الشرط فتعم، فليس في كلامه ما. أنه يوهم إنما يمضيه إذا كان موافقاً لرأيه كما زعم الشارح وفي الجامع الصغير وما اختلف فيه الفقهاء فقضى به القاضي ثم جاء قاض آخر يرى غير ذلك أمضاه . وفي المعراج وإنما ذكر لفظ الجامع بهذا اللفظ المذكور لأنه فيه فائدتين إحداهما أنه قيد بالفقهاء لأن القاضي إذا كان غير عالم بموضع الاجتهاد فاتفق قضاؤه في موضع الاجتهاد فعلى قول عامة المشايخ لا يجب على الثاني تنفيذه. كذا ذكره في فصول الأستروشني محالاً إلى المحيط والذخيرة فقال : لو قضى في فصل مجتهد فيه وهو لا يعلم بذلك، قيل ينفذ قضاؤه وعامتهم لا ينفذ وإنما ينفذ إذا علم بكونه مجتهداً فيه. قال شمس الأئمة : هذا هو ظاهر المذهب. والثاني أنه قيده بقوله «يرى غير ذلك» وفي رواية القدوري لم يتعرض لذلك فيحتمل أن قوله أمضاه» فيما إذا كان موافقاً وفي الخلاصة: إن هذا الشرط يعني كونه عالماً بالاختلاف وإن كان ظاهر المذهب لكن يفتي بخلافه. وشمل قوله «حكم قاض» ما إذا كان الحكم موافقاً لرأيه أو مخالفاً وما إذا كان القاضي باقياً على قضائه أو مات أو عزل كما الجمعة أفسد صلاته ثم افتتح بهم قوله : (أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان موافقاً لرأيه) أي لرأي القاضي المرفوع إليه حكم القاضي الأول قوله : (لكون لفظ الحكم نكرة في سياق الشرط فتعم) فيه نظر فإن ذلك إذا كان الشرط يميناً مثبتاً مثل إن كلمت رجلاً فكذا فإن المعنى لا أكلم رجلاً فتكون النكرة في سياق النفي معنى فتعم بخلاف قولك اليمين المنفي مثل إن لم أكلم رجلاً فلا : تعم لأنه في معنى لأكلمن رجلاً فهي نكرة في سياق الإثبات، وبخلاف الشرط الواقع غير يمين مثل إن جاءك رجل فأكرمه فإنه أيضاً غير نص في العموم لأنه في سياق الإثبات، وما في المتن من هذا القبيل كما لا يخفى
His saying: (And if a ruling of a judge is presented to him, he enforces it unless it contradicts the Book, the well-known Sunna, and consensus) because the first ijtihad is strengthened by the judgment and thus cannot be overturned. He made it absolute, so it includes whether it agrees with his opinion or disagrees, because the word 'ruling' is indefinite in the context of a condition, thus [it indicates] generality. So there is nothing in his words that suggests that he only enforces it if it agrees with his opinion, as the commentator claimed. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: And what the jurists differed over, and a judge judged by it, then another judge came who held a different view, he enforces it. And in al-Mi'raj: He only mentioned the phrase 'al-jami' with this wording because it contains two benefits: one is that he restricted it to jurists because if the judge is unaware of the place of ijtihad and his judgment coincides with a place of ijtihad, then according to the general opinion of the mashayikh, the second is not required to enforce it. Thus it is mentioned in Fusul al-Asturushi, referring to al-Muhit and al-Dhakhira: He said: If he judged on a matter of ijtihad while he does not know that, it is said his judgment is enforced, but the majority do not enforce it; rather it is enforced only if he knows it to be a matter of ijtihad. Shams al-A'imma said: This is the apparent madhhab. The second is that he restricted it by his saying 'he holds a different view.' And in the narration of al-Quduri, he did not address that, so it is possible that his saying 'he enforces it' is when it agrees. And in al-Khulasa: This condition means he is knowledgeable of the difference [of opinion], even if the apparent madhhab is that it is given contrary. And his saying 'a judge's ruling' includes whether the ruling agrees with his opinion or disagrees, and whether the judge remains on his judgment or dies or is removed, like the example of Friday: he corrupted his prayer then led them. His saying: (He made it absolute, so it includes whether it agrees with his opinion) i.e., the opinion of the judge to whom the first judge's ruling is presented. His saying: (Because the word 'ruling' is indefinite in the context of a condition, thus it gives generality) is questionable, because that applies if the condition is a positive oath like 'if I speak to a man', meaning 'I will not speak to a man', so the indefinite in the context of negation gives generality, unlike your saying a negative oath like 'if I do not speak to a man', which does not give generality because it is in the meaning of 'I will surely speak to a man', so it is an indefinite in the context of affirmation. And unlike a condition that is not an oath, like 'if a man comes to you, honor him', which is also not explicit in generality because it is in the context of affirmation. And what is in the text is of this type, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, explaining the ruling that a second judge must enforce the first judge's ruling unless it contradicts the Quran, well-known Sunna, or consensus. The commentator first establishes that the wording is absolute, covering both conformity and non-conformity with the second judge's own view, because 'a ruling' is indefinite in a conditional context, which indicates generality. He then rebuts a claim that the first judge's ruling is only enforced if it agrees with the second judge's opinion. He cites al-Jami' al-Saghir to support the generality. Next, he notes a nuance: if the first judge was unaware that the matter was open to ijtihad, most scholars say the second judge need not enforce it. He quotes Shams al-A'imma affirming this as the apparent madhhab. He also discusses a second interpretation that restricts the enforcement to cases where the second judge sees the issue differently. The passage then critiques a grammatical argument about the indefinite in conditional phrases, explaining that the text's wording (a condition that is not an oath) does not automatically entail generality. This is a detailed usuli discussion about the scope of enforcement.

Key Terms
حكم (hukm): A judge's ruling or judgment on a legal case.
قاض (qadi): A judge appointed to adjudicate disputes.
مجتهد فيه (mujtahid fihi): A legal issue that is open to ijtihad, where scholars may differ.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi majority in this passage, when is the second judge NOT required to enforce the first judge's ruling?
A) When the first judge was unaware the matter was open to ijtihad.
B) When the ruling differs from the second judge's opinion.
C) When the first judge has died.
D) When the ruling is based on a weak opinion.

Key Takeaway
The second judge must enforce the first judge's ruling unless the ruling contradicts qat'i sources or the first judge was ignorant of the ijtihad nature.

Qada — Judiciary

The Muqallid Judge and His Madhhab: When Does a Ruling Stand?
Discusses the effect of a muqallid judge's ruling when it contradicts his own madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the enforcement of a muqallid judge's ruling when it contradicts his madhhab, and distinguish the factors that affect validity (deliberate vs forgetful, knowledge of ikhtilaf).

Core Idea
A muqallid judge's authority is limited to his madhhab; violating it may void his ruling, but later authorities differ on enforcement.

The role of a judge in Islamic law is to adjudicate disputes based on the principles of the school he represents. But what happens when a non-mujtahid judge, appointed to follow Abu Hanifa, issues a ruling that goes against his own madhhab? The Hanafi school contains a rich debate on whether such a ruling is enforceable or null, with implications for legal stability and the limits of a muqallid's authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Recognize that the passage addresses the validity (nafadh) of a muqallid judge's ruling when he departs from his appointed madhhab. This is distinct from the case of a mujtahid judge.
The muqallid is bound to follow Abu Hanifa.
2
Distinguish the main positions
The text presents two views: Muhammad holds the ruling is enforced and cannot be annulled; a second view (from al-Imadi and others) says it is not enforced. Note that both agree on the judge being considered dismissed for violation.
The disagreement is on the effect of the ruling itself.
3
Refine with the deliberate vs forgetful factor
The passage further splits the issue: if the judge knowingly (deliberately) rules against his madhhab, some sources claim consensus that the ruling is invalid; others report a difference. If forgetful, some allow enforcement.
Consensus claim is contested.
4
Consider the role of following another valid opinion
Later authorities (e.g., in al-Bahr) suggest that if the muqallid follows another mujtahid's sound opinion or a weak narration, the ruling may still be enforced for legal stability.
This expands the scope of permissible departure.
5
Determine the practical thamarah
The practical consequence: a party who won a case under such a ruling may or may not be secure. The absence of a single tarjih means later practice leans toward non-enforcement for deliberate violations but enforcement if a valid alternative was followed.
Mufti should assess the specific circumstances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما المقلد فإنما ولاه ليحكم بمذهب أبي حنيفة فلا يملك المخالفة فيكون معزولاً ثم اعلم أن عبارات المشايخ قد اختلفت في هذه المسألة أعني ما بالنسبة إلى ذلك الحكم ا هـ . إذا قضى المقلد بخلاف مذهبه موافقاً لمذهب مجتهد ; ففي البزازية معزياً إلى شرح الطحاوي إذا لم يكن القاضي مجتهداً وقضى بالفتوى ثم تبين أنه على خلاف مذهبه نفذ وليس لغيره نقضه محمد. وقال الثاني: ليس له أن ينقضه أيضاً ا ينقضه. كذا العمادي في الفصول. ثم قال : القاضي إذا قضى في محل الاجتهاد وهو يرى خلاف ذلك في بعض المواضع أنه لا ينفذ وفي بعضها أنه ينفذ ولم يذكر فيه خلافاً، والصحيح أن فيه أبي حنيفة وصاحبيه . وذكر في المحيط اختلاف الرواية في بعضها في نفاذ القضاء وفي بعضها في حل الإقدام على القضاء ا هـ . وله خلافاً بين عن ه. وهكذا ذكر وفي عمدة الفتاوى : القاضي إذا قضى بقول مرجوع عنه جاز، وكذا لو قضى في الذي رفع حكمه إلى القاضي الثاني ثم إن هذه المسألة غير ما قبلها فإن ما قبلها هو اشتراط كون القاضي الأول عالماً بالخلاف لينفذ حكمه، وهذه في اشتراط كونه حكم على وفق مذهبه لا إذا كان ناسياً وحكم على مذهب غيره . قوله : (وفي العامد (روايتان كأنه اقتصر في معين الحكام على رواية عدم النفاذ فحكى الإجماع حيث قال : كما نقله الرملي عند ذكر القاضي أبي بكر الرازي رحمه الله تعالى الخلاف فيما إذا قضى بخلاف مذهبه وقد نسيه وأما إذا قضى بخلاف مذهبه حال ذكر مذهبه لا يجوز حكمه بالإجماع اهـ. لكن في الشرنبلالية نقل عن شرح الجامع الكبير أنه العامد لا خلاف بين أصحابنا قال : والخلاف ثابت على الصحيح قوله : (والوجه في هذا الزمان (الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية نقل هذا في البرهان عن قال : وهذا صريح الحق الذي يعض عليه بالنواجذ قوله : (ثم اعلم أن عبارات المشايخ قد اختلفت الخ) قال في النهر بعدما مر آنفاً عن الفتح : وهو ظاهر في أن كونه عالماً بالخلاف إنما هو في القاضي المجتهد. وفي القنية : القاضي المقلد إذا قضى بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ. وادعى في البحر أن المقلد إذا قضى بمذهب غيره أو برواية ضعيفة أو بقول ضعيف نفذ وأقوى ما تمسك به ما في الكمال ثم
As for the muqallid, he was appointed only to rule according to the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, so he has no authority to violate it; thus he is considered dismissed. Then know that the expressions of the mashayikh differ on this issue, meaning regarding that ruling. If the muqallid judged contrary to his madhhab, agreeing with a mujtahid's madhhab: In al-Bazzaziyya by reference to Sharh al-Tahawi: If the judge is not a mujtahid and he judged according to a fatwa, then it becomes clear that it was contrary to his madhhab, it is enforced and no one else can annul it, according to Muhammad. And the second said: He also cannot annul it. Thus al-Imadi in al-Fusul. Then he said: The judge if he ruled on a matter of ijtihad while he holds a contrary view, in some places it is not enforced and in others it is enforced, and he did not mention a difference [of opinion]; the correct is that there is [difference] between Abu Hanifa and his two companions. And he mentioned in al-Muhit the variation in transmissions: in some regarding the enforcement of the judgment and in some regarding the permissibility of proceeding with the judgment. And similarly mentioned in 'Umdat al-Fatawa: The judge if he judged by an abandoned opinion, it is permissible; likewise if he judged in the case that his judgment was referred to the second judge. Then indeed this issue is different from the previous one, because the previous one was about the condition that the first judge be knowledgeable of the difference for his ruling to be enforced, while this is about the condition that he ruled according to his own madhhab, not when he was forgetful and ruled by another madhhab. His saying: (And regarding the deliberate, there are two narrations). It seems he restricted it in Mu'in al-Hukkam to the narration of non-enforcement and reported consensus, saying: As al-Ramli reported, when mentioning the judge Abu Bakr al-Razi, there is difference of opinion when he judged contrary to his madhhab and had forgotten it. But if he judged contrary to his madhhab while remembering his madhhab, his judgment is not permissible by consensus. However, in al-Shurnublaliyya, it is reported from Sharh al-Jami' al-Kabir that regarding the deliberate, there is no difference among our associates; he said: And the difference is established as correct. His saying: (And the appropriate view in this time...) He said in al-Shurnublaliyya: This is reported in al-Burhan from... He said: This is the clear truth that is to be held onto strongly. His saying: (Then know that the expressions of the mashayikh differ...) He said in al-Nahr after what has passed earlier from al-Fath: It is apparent that being knowledgeable of the difference is only required for the mujtahid judge. And in al-Quniyya: The muqallid judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab, it is not enforced. And it is claimed in al-Bahr that the muqallid if he judges by another madhhab or a weak narration or a weak opinion, it is enforced; and the strongest evidence they hold is what is in al-Kamal.
Source pages p. 15

Commentary
This passage focuses on the muqallid (non-mujtahid) judge. It begins by stating that a muqallid is appointed specifically to rule by the Hanafi madhhab, so he cannot validly rule against it; if he does, he is considered dismissed from office. Then it notes that the mashayikh differ on the effect of such a ruling. It presents a view from Sharh al-Tahawi that if a muqallid rules contrary to his madhhab but according to some other fatwa, the ruling is enforced and no one can annul it (according to Muhammad). Another view (second) says it also cannot be annulled. The passage then discusses that when a judge rules on a matter of ijtihad while holding a different view, there is variation in enforcement. It distinguishes this issue from the previous one: earlier the condition was that the first judge must be aware of the ikhtilaf; now the condition is that he must have ruled according to his own madhhab unless forgetful. Next, it addresses the deliberate violation: some sources claim consensus that if he remembers his madhhab and rules against it, the ruling is invalid; but other sources report that there is no such consensus and the ikhtilaf is real. Finally, it cites later authorities: in al-Quniyya, a muqallid's ruling against his madhhab is not enforced; but in al-Bahr, it is enforced, especially if he followed another mujtahid's opinion or a weak narration. The strongest evidence for the latter is from al-Kamal. Overall, this shows a range of opinions within the Hanafi school on the validity of a muqallid's ruling when he departs from his appointed madhhab.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid): A judge who is not a mujtahid and is bound to follow the rulings of a particular madhhab (e.g., Abu Hanifa).
نفاذ (nafadh): Enforcement or validity of a judicial ruling.
عامد (amid): Deliberate; the judge knowingly violates his madhhab while remembering it.
نأسي (nasi): Forgetful; the judge rules contrary to his madhhab due to forgetfulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must be knowledgeable in all schools of thought.
B) He is appointed to rule only according to the Hanafi madhhab.
C) He can rule by any madhhab as long as he is just.
D) He must be a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
A muqallid judge's authority is strictly tied to his appointed madhhab; violation may void his ruling, but later authorities differ on enforcement depending on deliberation and the validity of the alternative opinion followed.

Qada — Judiciary
