Enforceability of a Judge's Ruling Contrary to His Madhhab
Intra-Hanafi dispute on enforceability of a qadi's ruling contra his school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on a muqallid judge's ruling contra his madhhab, and apply the relied-upon distinction between intentional and mistaken rulings.

Core Idea
A muqallid judge's contrary ruling enforces if intentional but not if mistaken.

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when does a judge's ruling that contradicts his own madhhab become enforceable? The text draws on the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and later authorities, culminating in a practical distinction that governs Hanafi judges today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
A judge rules contrary to his madhhab — is the ruling enforceable (yanfudh) or not? This is the central question.
The act is judicial ruling (qada); the ruling is about its validity.
2
Locate the ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that it enforces; Abu Yusuf holds that it does not. This is the basic conflict.
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) generally follow Abu Yusuf.
3
Note the qualification by judge type
The dispute is specifically for a muqallid (non-mujtahid) judge. For a mujtahid judge, the ruling is enforceable by consensus because he is presumed to have exercised ijtihad.
A mujtahid's ruling cannot be overturned by another mujtahid.
4
Analyze the tarjih (preferred view)
Later authorities (e.g., Fath al-Qadir, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) distinguish between intentional and mistaken rulings. If the judge intentionally follows another madhhab, the ruling is enforceable; if he mistakenly thought it was his madhhab, it is invalid.
This is the mufta bihi (operative) position.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical effect: A Hanafi judge may enforce a ruling based on Shafi'i or Maliki opinion if he does so intentionally, and the ruling stands; but if he errs, it is overturned.
This flexibility allows for pragmatic judicial decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب كتاب القاضي إلى القاضي وغيره في فصل مجتهد فيه ا هـ . وكذا في السراجية. وفي مآل الفتاوى : قضى بخلاف مذهبه وهو مختلف فيه قال أبو حنيفة ينفذ، وقال أبو يوسف لا ينفذ ا هـ. فقد تحرر أن القاضي المقلد إذا قضى بمذهب غيره فإنه ينفذ، وكذا إذا قضى برواية ضعيفة أو بقول ضعيف لإطلاق قولهم إن القول الضعيف يتقوى بقضاء القاضي، وما قيده به في فتح القدير من أن هذا إنما هو المجتهد ثابت في بعض العبارات، ولذا قال في القنية : القاضي المقلد إذا قضى بخلاف مذهبه لا ينفذ ا هـ. ويخالفه ما أفتى به شيخه الشيخ عمر قارئ الهداية حين سئل عن وقف لم يحكم به رجع الواقف عنه ووقفه على جهة أخرى وحكم به قاض حنفي، فهل يصح الثاني أم الأول؟ أجاب بأن الثاني هو الصحيح وإن كان الفتوى على خلاف قول أبي حنيفة لكنه تأيد بحكم الحاكم. وفي شرح منظومة ابن وهبان له صورة المسألة : لو حكم الحاكم في واقعة بحكم بخلاف مذهب مقلده - بفتح اللام - يعني الإمام الذي يقلده، وهذا إذا كان القاضي مقلداً وليس هو من أهل الاجتهاد كالقضاة الحنفية في زماننا مثلاً هل يصح قضاؤه أو لا؟ والجواب أنه إن كان ذاكراً لمذهبه لا يجوز وإلا جاز عنده خلافاً لهما ا هـ. ومن العجيب أن صاحب البدائع قيد الخلاف بعكس ما في فتح القدير فقال : ما نقل أن القاضي إذا قضى بخلاف مذهبه عمداً وقع باطلاً، وإن كان ناسياً عنده يصح، وعندهما لا يصح وهذا إذا كان القاضي ليس من أهل الاجتهاد، فأما إذا كان من أهل الاجتهاد فينبغي أن يصح قضاؤه في الحكم بالإجماع ولا يكون لقاض آخر أن يبطله لأنه لا يصدق على النسيان بل يحمل على أنه اجتهد فأدى اجتهاده إلى مذهب خصمه فقضى به فيكون قضاؤه باجتهاده فيصح ا هـ البزازية : إذا لم يكن القاضي مجتهداً وقضى بالفتوى الخ. وما في الفتح يجب أن يعوّل عليه في المذهب، وما في البزازية محمول على رواية عنهما إذ قصارى الأمر أن هذا منزل منزلة الناسي لمذهبه وقد مر عنهما في المجتهد أنه لا ينفذ فالمقلد أولى قوله : (يعني) الإمام الذي يقلده) كان عليه أن يقول الذي يقلده هو بزيادة الضمير العائد إلى القاضي والحاصل أن المراد به مذهب المجتهد كأبي حنيفة والشافعي مثلاً لا السلطان المقلد - بكسر اللام - قوله : ( ومن العجب أن صاحب البدائع قيد الخلاف الخ) حاصله أن صاحب البدائع جعل الخلاف في نفاذ قضائه بخلاف مذهبه وعدم نفاذه في القاضي غير المجتهد عكس ما صرح به في الفتح بقوله : هذا كله في القاضي المجتهد. وقول الرملي ما تقدم من قوله وفي فتح القدير: لا يظهر منه أنه عكسه، وكذا قوله وما قيده به في فتح القدير فتأمل غير ظاهر ثم إن ما ذكره في البدائع وجيه فإن المجتهد إذا حكم بخلاف مذهبه عمداً كان ذلك رجوعاً عن مذهبه الأوّل لتغير اجتهاده ووجوب اتباع ما ظهر له ثانياً، وإن لم يعلم أنه خالفه عمداً يحمل عليه لا على أنه نسي مذهبه وحكم بخلافه إذ الأصل أنه فعل ذلك عمداً وهذا معنى قول البدائع . لأنه لا يصدق على النسيان الخ. اهـ . لا مطلقاً بلفظه . والحق في هذه المسألة أن القاضي إذا حكم على خلاف مذهبه، فإن كان متوهماً أنه على وفقه فإنه باطل يجب نقضه وإن وافق مجتهداً فيه، وإن كان معتمداً مذهب غيره فإنه لا ينقض، وهذا التفصيل متعين في حكام زماننا فإنهم لا يعتمدون في أحكامهم على الاجتهاد ولا مقيداً لكونهم مقلدين، فإذا جرى منهم الحكم بخلاف مذهبهم فهو مقطوع بكونه منه خطأ فينقض . وقولهم لا ينقض الحكم في المجتهدات معلل بأن الاجتهاد لا ينقض بمثله لا مطلقاً فإذا كان القاضي متوهماً أنه مذهبه فأخطأ فيه لم يكن مجتهداً فيه. ومعنى قوله «أمضاه» حكم بمقتضاه . وفي السراج الوهاج : المراد من الحاكم القاضي والمراد من الإمضاء إلزام الحكم بعد دعوى صحيحة من خصم على خصم ولذا قال في البزازية : وإن أرادوا أن يثبتوا حكم الخليفة عند الأصل لا بد من تقديم دعوى صحيحة على خصم حاضر وإقامة البينة كما لو أرادوا إثبات قضاء قاض آخر ا هـ اهـ. أن يصير فالحاصل أن الحكم المرفوع لا بد أن يكون في حادثة وخصومة صحيحة كما صرح به العمادي في الفصول والبزازي في الفتاوى قالا : وهنا شرط لنفاذ القضاء في المجتهدات وهو حادثة تجري بين يدي القاضي من خصم على خصم حتى لو فات هذا الشرط لا ينفذ القضاء لأنه فتوى ا هـ . فلو رفع إلى حنفي قضاء مالكي بلا دعوى لم يلتفت إليه ويحكم بمقتضى مذهبه، ولا بد في إمضاء الثاني لحكم الأول من الدعوى أيضاً كما سمعت، ولا يشترط إحضار شهود الأصل بل يكفي على قضاء القاضي قال في البزازية : قاضي بلدة حكم على رجل بمال وسجل ثم مات القاضي وأحضر المدعي المحكوم عليه عند قاض آخر وبرهن على قضاء الأول أجبره الثاني على أداء المال إن كان الحكم الأول صحيحاً، ولو شهدوا أن قاضياً من قضاة البلد قضى بهذا المال لا يحكم به، وفي كل فعل لا بد من تسمية الفاعل ونسبه، فإن قال الشهود إن القاضي الأول غير عدل لا يمضي القاضي الثاني قضاءه ا هـ . وكتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أن القاضي إذا ارتاب في حكم الأول له أن يطلب شهود الأصل، وإذا علمت ذلك ظهر لك أن التنافيذ الواقعة في زماننا غير معتبرة لصدورها بلا دعوى وحادثة. وإنما يقيم صاحب الواقعة بينة يشهدون على حكم القاضي فلان ليكتب له القاضي الثاني أنه اتصل به حكم الأول ونفذه فإن قلت : القاضي إذا قضى بشيء في حادثة بعد قوله : (وقولهم لا ينقض الحكم في المجتهدات (الخ قال الرملي يجب أن يقيد ذلك بما إذا لم يقيده السلطان بمذهب، أما إذا قيده بمذهب كما إذا قال له أو كتب في منشوره وليتك لتحكم بين الناس بما صح أو بالصحيح من مذهب أبي حنيفة مثلاً فلا ينفذ قضاؤه بغيره لما تقرر أن القضاء يتخصص بالزمان والمكان والأشخاص والحوادث فتنبه لذلك قوله : ( وكتبنا في الفوائد الفقهية أن القاضي الخ) نقله في النهر عن المؤلف ثم قال : ولم أجده لغيره قوله : (التنافيذ الواقعة الخ) قال البحر الرائق ج۷/ م ٢
Book of Transfer (Hawalah), Chapter on the Judge's Letter to Another Judge etc. Section on a matter subject to ijtihad, end. And similarly in al-Sirajiyyah. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: He judged contrary to his madhhab, and it is disputed: Abu Hanifa said it enforces, and Abu Yusuf said it does not enforce, end. So it is established that the muqallid judge if he judges by someone else's madhhab, it enforces, and similarly if he judges by a weak narration or a weak opinion, because of the absolute nature of their statement that a weak opinion is strengthened by the judge's ruling. And what was qualified in Fath al-Qadir that this is only for the mujtahid is established in some expressions. Hence it is said in al-Qunyah: The muqallid judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab, it does not enforce, end. And it is contradicted by what his teacher Shaykh Umar Qari' al-Hidayah gave as a fatwa when asked about a waqf that was not enforced, the waqif returned from it and made a waqf for another purpose, and a Hanafi judge judged by it. Which is valid, the second or the first? He answered that the second is correct, even if the fatwa is contrary to Abu Hanifa's saying, but it is supported by the judge's ruling. And in Sharh Manzumah Ibn Wahban, he has the scenario: If a ruler judges in a matter by a ruling contrary to the madhhab of his muqallid (with fatha on lam) meaning the imam he follows, and this refers to when the judge is a muqallid and not from the people of ijtihad, like the Hanafi judges in our time, for example, does his judgment become valid or not? The answer is that if he is mindful of his madhhab it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible according to him, contrary to the two. End. And it is strange that the author of al-Bada'i' qualified the difference opposite to what is in Fath al-Qadir, saying: What is narrated that the judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab intentionally, it occurs as invalid, and if he is forgetful, it is valid according to him, and according to them it is not valid. And this is when the judge is not from the people of ijtihad. As for when he is from the people of ijtihad, it should be that his judgment is valid in the ruling by consensus, and no other judge may invalidate it, because he is not considered to be forgetful, but rather it is interpreted that he exercised ijtihad and his ijtihad led him to the madhhab of his opponent, so he judged by it, so his judgment based on his ijtihad is valid. End. al-Bazzaziyyah: If the judge is not a mujtahid and he judges by the fatwa etc. And what is in al-Fath must be relied upon in the madhhab, and what is in al-Bazzaziyyah is carried on a narration from them given that the utmost of the matter is that this is treated as the forgetful of his madhhab and it has passed from them regarding the mujtahid that it does not enforce, so the muqallid is more appropriate. His statement (meaning the imam he follows) he should say 'the one he follows' with an additional pronoun referring to the judge. The result is that what is meant is the madhhab of the mujtahid like Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i, for example, not the ruler who is muqallid (with kasra). His statement (And it is strange that the author of al-Bada'i' qualified the difference etc.) The summary is that the author of al-Bada'i' made the difference regarding enforcement and non-enforcement of his judgment contrary to his madhhab in the case of the non-mujtahid judge, opposite to what was stated in al-Fath in his saying: This is all regarding the mujtahid judge. And the statement of al-Ramli: 'What has preceded from his saying in Fath al-Qadir does not show that it is its opposite, and similarly his saying 'and what he qualified in Fath al-Qadir' reflect, is not apparent. Then what he mentioned in al-Bada'i' is sound, for if the mujtahid judges contrary to his madhhab intentionally, that is a retreat from his first madhhab due to change in his ijtihad and the necessity of following what appears to him secondly, and if it is not known that he intentionally violated it, it is interpreted as such, not that he forgot his madhhab and judged contrary to it, because the presumption is that he did it intentionally, and this is the meaning of al-Bada'i's statement: 'because he is not considered to be forgetful' etc. End. Not absolutely by its wording. And the truth in this issue is that the judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab, if he mistakenly thought it was in accordance with it, then it is invalid and must be overturned even if it agrees with a mujtahid therein, and if he intentionally followed another madhhab, then it is not overturned. This detail is necessary for the judges of our time, for they do not rely in their judgments on ijtihad nor are they bound because they are muqallids. So if they issue a judgment contrary to their madhhab, it is definitely an error from them and so is overturned. Their statement 'the judgment is not overturned in matters of ijtihad' is reasoned by that ijtihad is not overturned by its like, not absolutely. So if the judge mistakenly thought it was his madhhab and erred in it, he was not a mujtahid in it. And the meaning of his statement 'he enforced it' is he judged by its requirement. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The meaning of al-hakim is the judge, and the meaning of al-imda' is the enforcement of the judgment after a valid claim from one litigant against another. Hence it is said in al-Bazzaziyyah: If they want to establish the judgment of the caliph regarding the original, it is necessary to present a valid claim against a present litigant and to provide evidence, just as if they wanted to establish the judgment of another judge. End. Thus the result is that the judgment raised must be regarding an incident and a valid dispute, as stated by al-Imadi in al-Fusul and al-Bazzazi in al-Fatawa. They said: Here is a condition for the enforcement of judgment in matters of ijtihad, which is an incident that occurs before the judge from one litigant against another, such that if this condition is missing, the judgment does not enforce because it is a fatwa. End. So if a Maliki judgment is raised to a Hanafi without a claim, it is not considered and he judges according to his madhhab. And it is necessary in the enforcement by the second judge of the first's judgment to have a claim as well, as you heard. And it is not required to bring the original witnesses, rather testifying upon the judgment of the judge is sufficient. It is said in al-Bazzaziyyah: A judge of a town judged against a man for property and recorded it, then the judge died, and the claimant brought the defendant before another judge and proved the first judgment, the second compels him to pay the property if the first judgment was valid. And if they testified that a judge from the judges of the town judged this property, he does not judge by it. And in every action, it is necessary to name the doer and his lineage. If the witnesses say that the first judge is not just, the second judge does not enforce his judgment. End. And we wrote in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that if the judge doubts the first judgment, he may request the original witnesses. And when you know that, it becomes clear that the enforcements occurring in our time are not considered because they come without a claim or incident. Rather, the person with the incident brings witnesses to testify to the judgment of judge so-and-so so that the second judge can write that the first judgment has reached him and he enforces it. If you say: The judge if he judges something in an incident after his statement (And their statement 'the judgment is not overturned in matters of ijtihad' etc.) al-Ramli said: It must be qualified by when the ruler has not restricted him to a madhhab. As for when he restricted him to a madhhab, as when he said to him or wrote in his letter of appointment 'I have appointed you to judge between people according to what is correct or according to the correct view of Abu Hanifa's madhhab, for example', then his judgment by other than it does not enforce because it is established that judgment is specialized by time, place, persons, and incidents. So pay attention to that. His statement (And we wrote in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that the judge etc.) He narrated it in al-Nahr from the author then said: I did not find it from others. His statement (the enforcements occurring etc.) He said al-Bahr al-Ra'iq j7 m2
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the enforceability of a judge's ruling when he judges contrary to his own madhhab. The author begins by stating the basic difference: Abu Hanifa holds that such a ruling is enforceable (yanfudh), while Abu Yusuf says it is not. The majority of later authorities (like al-Sirajiyyah and Ma'al al-Fatawa) affirm that a muqallid judge's ruling by another madhhab, a weak narration, or a weak opinion is enforceable because the judge's ruling strengthens the weak opinion. However, Fath al-Qadir qualifies this as only for a mujtahid judge. Al-Qunyah states the opposite for a muqallid. The author then presents a fatwa from Shaykh Umar Qari' al-Hidayah which supports enforceability even against Abu Hanifa's view, because the judge's ruling (hukm al-hakim) gives it effect. The discussion continues with the view from Sharh Manzumah Ibn Wahban that if the muqallid judge is mindful of his madhhab but rules against it intentionally, it is invalid; if unaware, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa but not the Sahibayn. The author of al-Bada'i' reverses the qualification compared to Fath al-Qadir: for a non-mujtahid, intentional violation invalidates; forgetfulness makes it valid according to Abu Hanifa but not them. For a mujtahid judge, his ruling is valid by consensus because he is presumed to have exercised ijtihad. The author then clarifies that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is from Fath al-Qadir. Finally, he gives the correct position: if the judge mistakenly thought his ruling agreed with his madhhab, it is invalid and must be overturned; if he intentionally followed another madhhab, it is not overturned. He also discusses conditions for enforcement, such as the necessity of a valid claim (da'wa sahiha) and a dispute (khusuma) for the judgment to be binding, distinguishing it from a mere fatwa. The passage concludes with procedural details about proving the first judgment before the second judge.

Key Terms
القاضي المقلد (al-qadi al-muqallid): A judge who does not have the capacity for ijtihad and follows a specific madhhab (e.g., Hanafi).
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A scholar qualified to derive rulings directly from the primary sources.
إمضاء الحكم (imda' al-hukm): Enforcement or ratification of a previous judgment by a second judge.
القول الضعيف (al-qawl al-da'if): A weak opinion within the madhhab, less authoritative but still considered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position in this lesson, when is a muqallid judge's ruling contrary to his madhhab enforceable?
A) Only if he is forgetful of his madhhab
B) Only if he is a mujtahid
C) If he intentionally followed another madhhab
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A muqallid judge's ruling contrary to his madhhab is enforceable if intentional, but invalid if mistaken.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
7 min
Conditions for Enforcing a Judgment (Imda' al-Hukm)
Procedural conditions for enforcing a judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions required for a second judge to enforce a first judge's ruling, and explain why a judgment without a valid claim is considered a mere fatwa.

Core Idea
A judgment is only enforceable if it arises from a valid dispute and claim — otherwise it is a non-binding fatwa.

After establishing the enforceability of contrary rulings, the text turns to procedural requirements for imda' (enforcement) of a judgment. The key condition is that the ruling must be based on a specific incident and a valid legal claim between litigants. This lesson unpacks those conditions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the procedural requirement
The judgment must be based on a specific incident (haditha) and a valid dispute (khusuma) between litigants. Without these, the ruling is a fatwa, not a binding judgment.
This condition is cited from al-Imadi and al-Bazzazi.
2
Understand the condition for imda'
When a second judge enforces a first judgment, he must ensure there was a valid claim (da'wa sahiha). The first judgment must be proved by testimony of witnesses who attest to the judgment itself, not to the underlying facts.
Original witnesses are not required; testimony on the judgment suffices.
3
Apply to a scenario
If a Maliki judgment is presented to a Hanafi judge without a claim, he ignores it and applies his own madhhab. For imda', the second judge must also have a claim before him.
The second judge does not automatically enforce; a new claim is needed.
4
Note evidence rules
Witnesses must name the first judge and his lineage. If they say the first judge was unjust, the second judge does not enforce. If the second judge doubts, he may request original witnesses.
This ensures the authenticity and validity of the first judgment.
5
Consider restrictions by the ruler
If the ruler restricts the judge to a specific madhhab (e.g., 'judge by the correct view of Abu Hanifa'), then the judge cannot rule by another madhhab; such a judgment is unenforceable.
Judgment is specialized by time, place, persons, and incidents.

بعد قوله : (وقولهم لا ينقض الحكم في المجتهدات (الخ قال الرملي يجب أن يقيد ذلك بما إذا لم يقيده السلطان بمذهب، أما إذا قيده بمذهب كما إذا قال له أو كتب في منشوره وليتك لتحكم بين الناس بما صح أو بالصحيح ولم أجده لغيره قوله : (التنافيذ الواقعة الخ) قال البحر الرائق ج۷/ م ٢
Book of Transfer (Hawalah), Chapter on the Judge's Letter to Another Judge etc. Section on a matter subject to ijtihad, end. And similarly in al-Sirajiyyah. And in Ma'al al-Fatawa: He judged contrary to his madhhab, and it is disputed: Abu Hanifa said it enforces, and Abu Yusuf said it does not enforce, end. So it is established that the muqallid judge if he judges by someone else's madhhab, it enforces, and similarly if he judges by a weak narration or a weak opinion, because of the absolute nature of their statement that a weak opinion is strengthened by the judge's ruling. And what was qualified in Fath al-Qadir that this is only for the mujtahid is established in some expressions. Hence it is said in al-Qunyah: The muqallid judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab, it does not enforce, end. And it is contradicted by what his teacher Shaykh Umar Qari' al-Hidayah gave as a fatwa when asked about a waqf that was not enforced, the waqif returned from it and made a waqf for another purpose, and a Hanafi judge judged by it. Which is valid, the second or the first? He answered that the second is correct, even if the fatwa is contrary to Abu Hanifa's saying, but it is supported by the judge's ruling. And in Sharh Manzumah Ibn Wahban, he has the scenario: If a ruler judges in a matter by a ruling contrary to the madhhab of his muqallid (with fatha on lam) meaning the imam he follows, and this refers to when the judge is a muqallid and not from the people of ijtihad, like the Hanafi judges in our time, for example, does his judgment become valid or not? The answer is that if he is mindful of his madhhab it is not permissible, otherwise it is permissible according to him, contrary to the two. End. And it is strange that the author of al-Bada'i' qualified the difference opposite to what is in Fath al-Qadir, saying: What is narrated that the judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab intentionally, it occurs as invalid, and if he is forgetful, it is valid according to him, and according to them it is not valid. And this is when the judge is not from the people of ijtihad. As for when he is from the people of ijtihad, it should be that his judgment is valid in the ruling by consensus, and no other judge may invalidate it, because he is not considered to be forgetful, but rather it is interpreted that he exercised ijtihad and his ijtihad led him to the madhhab of his opponent, so he judged by it, so his judgment based on his ijtihad is valid. End. al-Bazzaziyyah: If the judge is not a mujtahid and he judges by the fatwa etc. And what is in al-Fath must be relied upon in the madhhab, and what is in al-Bazzaziyyah is carried on a narration from them given that the utmost of the matter is that this is treated as the forgetful of his madhhab and it has passed from them regarding the mujtahid that it does not enforce, so the muqallid is more appropriate. His statement (meaning the imam he follows) he should say 'the one he follows' with an additional pronoun referring to the judge. The result is that what is meant is the madhhab of the mujtahid like Abu Hanifa and al-Shafi'i, for example, not the ruler who is muqallid (with kasra). His statement (And it is strange that the author of al-Bada'i' qualified the difference etc.) The summary is that the author of al-Bada'i' made the difference regarding enforcement and non-enforcement of his judgment contrary to his madhhab in the case of the non-mujtahid judge, opposite to what was stated in al-Fath in his saying: This is all regarding the mujtahid judge. And the statement of al-Ramli: 'What has preceded from his saying in Fath al-Qadir does not show that it is its opposite, and similarly his saying 'and what he qualified in Fath al-Qadir' reflect, is not apparent. Then what he mentioned in al-Bada'i' is sound, for if the mujtahid judges contrary to his madhhab intentionally, that is a retreat from his first madhhab due to change in his ijtihad and the necessity of following what appears to him secondly, and if it is not known that he intentionally violated it, it is interpreted as such, not that he forgot his madhhab and judged contrary to it, because the presumption is that he did it intentionally, and this is the meaning of al-Bada'i's statement: 'because he is not considered to be forgetful' etc. End. Not absolutely by its wording. And the truth in this issue is that the judge if he judges contrary to his madhhab, if he mistakenly thought it was in accordance with it, then it is invalid and must be overturned even if it agrees with a mujtahid therein, and if he intentionally followed another madhhab, then it is not overturned. This detail is necessary for the judges of our time, for they do not rely in their judgments on ijtihad nor are they bound because they are muqallids. So if they issue a judgment contrary to their madhhab, it is definitely an error from them and so is overturned. Their statement 'the judgment is not overturned in matters of ijtihad' is reasoned by that ijtihad is not overturned by its like, not absolutely. So if the judge mistakenly thought it was his madhhab and erred in it, he was not a mujtahid in it. And the meaning of his statement 'he enforced it' is he judged by its requirement. And in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: The meaning of al-hakim is the judge, and the meaning of al-imda' is the enforcement of the judgment after a valid claim from one litigant against another. Hence it is said in al-Bazzaziyyah: If they want to establish the judgment of the caliph regarding the original, it is necessary to present a valid claim against a present litigant and to provide evidence, just as if they wanted to establish the judgment of another judge. End. Thus the result is that the judgment raised must be regarding an incident and a valid dispute, as stated by al-Imadi in al-Fusul and al-Bazzazi in al-Fatawa. They said: Here is a condition for the enforcement of judgment in matters of ijtihad, which is an incident that occurs before the judge from one litigant against another, such that if this condition is missing, the judgment does not enforce because it is a fatwa. End. So if a Maliki judgment is raised to a Hanafi without a claim, it is not considered and he judges according to his madhhab. And it is necessary in the enforcement by the second judge of the first's judgment to have a claim as well, as you heard. And it is not required to bring the original witnesses, rather testifying upon the judgment of the judge is sufficient. It is said in al-Bazzaziyyah: A judge of a town judged against a man for property and recorded it, then the judge died, and the claimant brought the defendant before another judge and proved the first judgment, the second compels him to pay the property if the first judgment was valid. And if they testified that a judge from the judges of the town judged this property, he does not judge by it. And in every action, it is necessary to name the doer and his lineage. If the witnesses say that the first judge is not just, the second judge does not enforce his judgment. End. And we wrote in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that if the judge doubts the first judgment, he may request the original witnesses. And when you know that, it becomes clear that the enforcements occurring in our time are not considered because they come without a claim or incident. Rather, the person with the incident brings witnesses to testify to the judgment of judge so-and-so so that the second judge can write that the first judgment has reached him and he enforces it. If you say: The judge if he judges something in an incident after his statement (And their statement 'the judgment is not overturned in matters of ijtihad' etc.) al-Ramli said: It must be qualified by when the ruler has not restricted him to a madhhab. As for when he restricted him to a madhhab, as when he said to him or wrote in his letter of appointment 'I have appointed you to judge between people according to what is correct or according to the correct view of Abu Hanifa's madhhab, for example', then his judgment by other than it does not enforce because it is established that judgment is specialized by time, place, persons, and incidents. So pay attention to that. His statement (And we wrote in al-Fawa'id al-Fiqhiyyah that the judge etc.) He narrated it in al-Nahr from the author then said: I did not find it from others. His statement (the enforcements occurring etc.) He said al-Bahr al-Ra'iq j7 m2
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the enforceability of a judge's ruling when he judges contrary to his own madhhab. The author begins by stating the basic difference: Abu Hanifa holds that such a ruling is enforceable (yanfudh), while Abu Yusuf says it is not. The majority of later authorities (like al-Sirajiyyah and Ma'al al-Fatawa) affirm that a muqallid judge's ruling by another madhhab, a weak narration, or a weak opinion is enforceable because the judge's ruling strengthens the weak opinion. However, Fath al-Qadir qualifies this as only for a mujtahid judge. Al-Qunyah states the opposite for a muqallid. The author then presents a fatwa from Shaykh Umar Qari' al-Hidayah which supports enforceability even against Abu Hanifa's view, because the judge's ruling (hukm al-hakim) gives it effect. The discussion continues with the view from Sharh Manzumah Ibn Wahban that if the muqallid judge is mindful of his madhhab but rules against it intentionally, it is invalid; if unaware, it is valid according to Abu Hanifa but not the Sahibayn. The author of al-Bada'i' reverses the qualification compared to Fath al-Qadir: for a non-mujtahid, intentional violation invalidates; forgetfulness makes it valid according to Abu Hanifa but not them. For a mujtahid judge, his ruling is valid by consensus because he is presumed to have exercised ijtihad. The author then clarifies that the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is from Fath al-Qadir. Finally, he gives the correct position: if the judge mistakenly thought his ruling agreed with his madhhab, it is invalid and must be overturned; if he intentionally followed another madhhab, it is not overturned. He also discusses conditions for enforcement, such as the necessity of a valid claim (da'wa sahiha) and a dispute (khusuma) for the judgment to be binding, distinguishing it from a mere fatwa. The passage concludes with procedural details about proving the first judgment before the second judge.

Key Terms
إمضاء الحكم (imda' al-hukm): Enforcement or ratification of a previous judgment by a second judge.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or complaint filed by a plaintiff against a defendant.
خصومة (khusuma): A legal dispute between two parties involving a claim and defense.
شهود الأصل (shuhud al-asl): Original witnesses who testify to the underlying facts of the case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if a judgment is rendered without a valid dispute (khusuma) between litigants?
A) It is still enforceable if the judge was a mujtahid
B) It is considered a fatwa and not enforceable
C) It is enforceable only if the parties agree
D) It is void ab initio

Key Takeaway
For a judgment to be enforceable, it must arise from a specific incident and a valid dispute; otherwise it is a mere fatwa.

Judge's Ruling on Necessary Consequences: Scope and Limits
Ruling on necessary consequences of a judge's verdict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a judge's ruling does not automatically cover necessary consequences unknown to the judge, and distinguish the binding effect of a prior judgment from later incidents.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling binds only what was before him; unknown consequences remain open for new ijtihad.

In Islamic judicial procedure, a judge's verdict carries authority. But does it implicitly nullify all related contracts and oaths, even those the judge never considered? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the Hanafi position through a series of examples involving suspended oaths and fuduli marriages. Understanding this nuance is crucial for practicing jurists and for anyone navigating the intersection of personal ijtihad and judicial binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The passage begins with a question: Does a judge's ruling cover necessary consequences he is unaware of? Identify the question and its immediate answer (No).
2
Extract the principle from al-Bazzaziyya
From the first example, note the condition: if the judge knows of the prior fuduli marriage, his ruling on the second marriage implies nullification of the oath and the fuduli marriage. If he does not know, his ruling does not affect them.
This establishes that knowledge is the key condition for coverage.
3
Analyse the case of the suspended oath and fuduli marriage
Trace the facts: a man marries through a fuduli, then divorces, then marries the woman himself. The judge's ruling depends on being informed of the prior marriage.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage reports a case from Imam Muhammad (the Second Imam). A man with a suspended oath marries while not believing it falls, and a judge rules the marriage valid. Later, the man changes his opinion. Imam Muhammad holds that the first marriage stands due to the judge's ruling, but for future marriages the man must follow his new opinion.
This shows a legitimate difference within the Hanafi school.
5
Understand the stratagem (hila)
The author provides a stratagem: the man can marry after rescinding the oath and claim the marriage is valid based on the earlier ruling, while the woman claims it is forbidden. A Hanafi judge will then rule according to Muhammad's view, making the marriage valid.
This illustrates the practical application of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دعوى هل يكون قضاء فيما هو من لوازمه وإن لم يعلم به القاضي؟ قلت : لا لما في قضاء البزازية في فصل فسخ اليمين المضافة : وإن زوّجه رجل امرأة بلا أمره وأجاز بالفعل ثم طلقها ثلاثاً ثم تزوجها بنفسه ثم ترافعا إلى القاضي فإن أعلمه بتقدم نكاح الفضولي فقضى بالنكاح صح ويكون قد قضى ببطلان اليمين وبطلان نكاح الفضولي وببطلان الثلاث بعده، وإن لم يعلم بتقدم النكاح يعلمه حتى يقضي في موضع الاجتهاد، ويقصد بالقضاء اليمين المضافة ونكاح الفضولي ا هـ . ثم قال : وروي عن الإمام الثاني فيمن قال كل امرأة يتزوجها فهي طالق فتزوج امرأة وهو لا يرى الوقوع فرفعته امرأته إلى قاض لا يرى الوقوع فقضى بصحة النكاح ثم تحوّل رأي الرجل إلى الوقوع فتزوج امرأة أخرى بعدها، فإنه يمسك الأول ويعمل برأيه الحادث في الحادثة فيفارقها لأن القاضي إنما قضى بإبطال الطلاق في الأولى بالاجتهاد فنفذ قضاؤه فبعد ذلك بتحول رأيه لا يملك نقض رأيه ذلك، وأما الحادثة فيثبت عليها الحل الآن ولم يجر عليها حكم القاضي فيعمل برأيه والحيلة فيه أن يتزوج امرأة بعد فسخ ويدعي عند القاضي أنها زوجته بحكم الفسخ على امرأة أخرى وتزعم المرأة أنها عليه حرام أخذاً بمذهب الثاني فيترافعان إلى القاضي الحنفي فيحكم القاضي الحنفي بأنها زوجته بمذهب محمد ا هـ . فقد علمت من ذلك كثيراً من المسائل،
Question: Does a ruling cover its necessary consequences even if the judge is unaware of them? I say: No, because of what is in al-Bazzaziyya in the section on rescission of the suspended oath: If a man marries a woman to someone without his permission and he subsequently ratifies it, then divorces her three times, then marries her himself, then they come to the judge: if he (the judge) is informed of the prior fuduli marriage and rules in favor of the marriage, it is valid, and he has ruled on the invalidity of the oath, the invalidity of the fuduli marriage, and the invalidity of the three divorces after it. But if he does not know of the prior marriage, he rules in the place of ijtihad, and he intends by his ruling the suspended oath and the fuduli marriage. End quote. Then he said: It is narrated from the Second Imam (Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a man who said, 'Every woman I marry is divorced,' then he married a woman while not believing in the occurrence (of divorce), so his wife brought him to a judge who also did not believe in occurrence, and he ruled the marriage valid. Then the man's opinion changed to occurrence, and he married another woman after that: He retains the first wife and acts on his new opinion in the new incident, separating from her (the second wife), because the judge only ruled on the annulment of the divorce in the first case by ijtihad, so his ruling is effective. Afterwards, by the change of his opinion, he cannot overturn that ruling. As for the new incident, the permissibility is proven now, and no ruling of the judge has passed on it, so he acts on his opinion. The stratagem is that he marries a woman after the rescission and claims before the judge that she is his wife by virtue of the annulment, and the woman claims that she is forbidden to him, adopting the doctrine of the second (Imam), so they take their case to the Hanafi judge, and the Hanafi judge rules that she is his wife according to the doctrine of Muhammad. End quote. So you have learned many issues from that.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a ruling_with_proof that addresses a critical procedural question: does a judge's ruling automatically cover all necessary legal consequences of that ruling, even if the judge himself was not aware of those consequences? The author, Ibn Nujaym, begins by stating the principle: No, a judge's ruling does not extend to consequences he was unaware of at the time of judgment. He supports this from al-Bazzaziyya. The example involves a marriage (nikah fuduli) and a suspended oath (yamin mudafa). If the judge knows of the prior fuduli marriage, his ruling on the second marriage implies nullification of the oath and the fuduli marriage. If he does not know, his ruling does not affect them. The passage then reports a case from Imam Muhammad: a man with a suspended oath marries a woman while neither he nor the judge believes the oath takes effect. Later, the man changes his opinion; his first marriage stands due to the judge's binding ruling, but for a subsequent marriage he must follow his new opinion. A stratagem (hila) is given: he can marry after rescinding the oath, then claim the marriage is valid based on the earlier ruling, while the woman claims it is forbidden, and a Hanafi judge will rule according to Muhammad's view. This demonstrates the nuanced operation of judicial rulings across different schools and personal opinions.

Key Terms
قضاء (qada): The judge's formal ruling on a case, which is binding and authoritative.
لوازم (lawa'zim): Necessary legal consequences that follow from the subject matter of a ruling, such as invalidity of related contracts.
اليمين المضافة (al-yamin al-mudafa): A suspended oath where divorce is conditioned on a future marriage.
نكاح الفضولي (nikah al-fuduli): A marriage contract made by a guardian without the permission of the person being married, but later ratified by that person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man with a suspended oath marries a woman and later his ijtihad changes, what is the status of his first marriage?
A) It becomes invalid because his opinion changed.
B) It remains valid because the judge's ruling on it was final.
C) It must be annulled by the judge again.
D) It is valid only if the woman agrees.

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling covers necessary consequences only if he was aware of them at the time of judgment.

The Scope of a Judge's Ruling: Pre-emption and Separable Consequences
Inter-madhhab dispute on the scope of a judge's ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle that a judge's ruling is limited to the specific case litigated and apply it to distinguish between rulings on a contract's validity and its separable consequences.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling on one matter does not automatically rule on other legal consequences not litigated.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a crucial procedural point: when a judge from one madhhab rules on a case, does that ruling bind other madhhabs on related but separate issues? The author uses the concept of 'haditha' (new incident) to argue that a ruling only covers what was actually claimed and proved. We will unpack the distinction between 'mujib' and 'muqtaḍā' and see how this affects pre-emption, lease termination, and other consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core debate
Determine whether a judge's ruling on a contract's validity extends to all its necessary effects across madhhabs.
2
Define key terms
Understand 'al-hukm bi al-mujib', 'mujib', 'muqtaḍā', and 'haditha' as used in the passage.
Al-mujib is the necessary effect; al-muqtaḍā is an inseparable attribute.
3
Analyze the examples
Examine the three cases: Shafi'i on sale and pre-emption, Hanafi on pre-emption, Maliki on suspended oath, and note what each ruling does NOT decide.
Each example shows a missing 'haditha'.
4
Understand the distinction
Differentiate between a ruling on the contract itself (validity) and a ruling on its effects (mujib) – the latter is a ruling on the party, not the contract.
Al-hukm bi al-mujib is a ruling on what is established, not on the contract's legal status.
5
Apply the principle
Conclude that each new claim requires a separate lawsuit; a prior ruling does not decide it.
This protects procedural fairness across madhhabs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا قضى شافعي بصحة : بيع الرملي : سيأتي أيضاً في شرح قوله وأمضى القاضي حكمه اهـ . قلت : وتقدم أيضاً في البحث السادس أول كتاب القضاء قوله: (فقضى بالنكاح) أي الثاني قوله : أن يتزوج امرأة بعد فسخ) أي بعد فسخ اليمين تأمل قوله : (فإذا قضى شافعي الخ) قال المؤلف في بعض رسائله وفي القاسمية : أما كون الحكم حادثة فاحتراز عما لم يحدث بعد كما لو حكم بموجب إجارة لا يكون حكماً بالفسخ بموت أحد المتآجرين، وكما له حكم بموجب بيع عقار لا يكون حكماً باستحقاق شفعة الجوار لأنه لم توجد فيه خصومة، وأما الخصومة الصحيحة فهي الدعوى المشتملة على شرائط الصحة اهـ. وذكر نصر بن نصر فيها أيضاً أن اشتراط تقدم الدعوى والحادثة مجمع عليه ثم قال فيها وقال الشيخ محب الدين أحمد الله البغدادي قاضي قضاة الحنابلة في رسالة وأما الحكم بالموجب - بفتح الجيم - فمعناه الحكم بموجب الدعوى الثابتة بالبينة أو غيرها. هذا هو معنى الموجب ولا معنى له غيره فينظر في الدعوى فإن كانت مشتملة على ما يقتضي صحة العقد المدعى به كان الحكم بموجبها حكماً بالصحة، وإن لم تشتمل على ما يقتضي صحة العقد المدعى به لم يكن الحكم حكماً بصحة العقد، والحكم بالموجب حكم على العاقد بما ثبت عليه من العقد لا حكم بالعقد .اهـ. وتمام ذلك في رسالة المؤلف ورأيت في كلام بعض المحققين من الشافعية أن الموجب عبارة عن الأثر المترتب على ذلك الشيء وهو والمقتضى مختلفان خلافاً لمن زعم اتحادهما إذ المتقضى لا ينفك والموجب قد ينفك، فالأول كانتقال الملك للمشتري بعد لزوم البيع، والثاني كالرد بالعيب والموجب أعم أي لأنه الأثر اللازم سواء كان ينفك أو لا. وذكر أن الحكم بالموجب يتضمن الحكم بالصحة لأنه لولا صحة العقد ما ترتبت عليه تلك عقار وموجبه لا يكون حكماً منه بأن لا شفعة للجار لعدم حادثتها ، وكذا إذا قضى حنفي لا يكون حكماً بأن الشفعة للجار وإن كانت الشفعة من مواجبه لأن حادثتها لم توجد وقت الحكم ولا شعور للقاضي بها، وكذا إذا قضى مالكي بصحة التعليق في اليمين المضافة لا يكون حكماً بأنه لا يصح نكاح الفضولي المجاز بالفعل لعدمه وقته فافهم فإن أكثر أهل الزمان عنه غافلون.
So if a Shafi'i judge rules on the validity of a sale of 'al-Ramli' property: this will also come in the commentary on his saying 'and the judge executed his ruling'. I say: and it has already preceded in the sixth investigation at the beginning of the Book of Judging his saying: ('he ruled on the validity of the marriage') meaning the second (marriage), his saying: 'that he marries a woman after rescission' meaning after the rescission of the oath. Consider his saying: ('So if a Shafi'i judge rules...'). The author said in some of his epistles and in al-Qasimiyya: As for the ruling being a new incident (haditha), it is a precaution against what has not yet occurred, like if he rules according to the necessary consequence of a lease, it is not a ruling on rescission due to the death of one of the lessors; and likewise if he rules according to the necessary consequence of a sale of real estate, it is not a ruling on entitlement to the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) of the neighbor because no litigation existed on it. As for valid litigation, it is a claim that meets the conditions of validity. End quote. And Nasr bin Nasr also mentioned in it that the condition of the prior existence of the claim and the incident is agreed upon. Then he said in it: And Shaykh Muhibb al-Din Ahmad Allah al-Baghdadi, the chief judge of the Hanbalis, said in an epistle: As for 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' (with fatha on the jim), its meaning is the ruling according to the necessary consequence of the claim established by evidence or otherwise. This is the meaning of 'mujib', and it has no other meaning. So one looks at the claim: if it includes what necessitates the validity of the contract claimed, then the ruling according to its necessary consequence is a ruling on validity; if it does not include what necessitates the validity of the claimed contract, then the ruling is not a ruling on the validity of the contract. And 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' is a ruling on the contracting party regarding what has been established against him of the contract, not a ruling on the contract. End quote. And the rest of that is in the author's epistle. And I saw in the speech of some verifying scholars of the Shafi'is that 'al-mujib' is the effect that follows from that thing, and it differs from 'al-muqtaḍā' (the required attribute), contrary to those who claim they are the same, because 'al-muqtaḍā' is inseparable, while 'al-mujib' may be separable. The first (muqtaḍā) is like the transfer of ownership to the buyer after the binding of the sale; the second (mujib) is like the option of return due to a defect. And 'al-mujib' is more general, i.e., because it is the necessary effect, whether it is separable or not. And he mentioned that 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' includes the ruling on validity because without the validity of the contract, those effects would not follow. So (if a Shafi'i judge rules on validity of sale of real estate), its necessary consequence does not constitute a ruling from him that there is no pre-emption for the neighbor because its incident (haditha) is absent; likewise, if a Hanafi judge rules, it is not a ruling that pre-emption is for the neighbor even though pre-emption is among its necessary consequences, because its incident did not exist at the time of the ruling and the judge has no awareness of it. Likewise, if a Maliki judge rules on the validity of a suspended oath (ta'liq), it is not a ruling that the fuduli marriage ratified by action is invalid because its incident (the ruling on that marriage) is absent at that time. So understand this, for most people today are heedless of it.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is an inter_madhhab_dispute expansion_probe that clarifies the scope of a judge's ruling across schools. The key issue: when a judge from one madhhab (e.g., Shafi'i) rules on a specific case, does that ruling automatically imply rulings on all related legal consequences (mujibat) according to other schools? The answer is no. The author explains using the concept of 'haditha' (new incident). A judge's ruling only applies to the specific controversy before him, not to potential consequences that were not litigated. This is illustrated with examples from qiyas: a Shafi'i judge ruling on a sale does not thereby rule on pre-emption (shuf'a) because pre-emption is a separate claim; a Hanafi judge ruling on a sale does not mean pre-emption is established; a Maliki judge ruling on a suspended oath does not rule on the validity of a subsequent fuduli marriage. The passage also provides an extensive definition of 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' from a Hanbali chief judge and a Shafi'i scholar. The 'mujib' is the legal effect that necessarily follows from a valid contract (like transfer of ownership), while 'muqtaḍā' is an inseparable attribute (like the option of defect). Ruling 'bi al-mujib' means ruling on the effect of the claim as proved, not on the contract itself; it implies validity because effects cannot arise from an invalid contract. However, a ruling on validity does not automatically rule on all specific effects unless they were claimed. This is a crucial procedural principle: to avoid assuming that a judge's ruling has unintended consequences across different madhhabs.

Key Terms
الحكم بالموجب (al-hukm bi al-mujib): Ruling according to the necessary consequence of the claim as established by evidence; it is a ruling on the effect, not directly on the contract.
الموجب (al-mujib): The necessary legal effect (e.g., transfer of ownership) that can be separable (like option of defect) or inseparable (like transfer).
المقتضى (al-muqtaḍā): An inseparable attribute or consequence (e.g., transfer of ownership upon sale) that cannot be detached.
حادثة (haditha): A new legal incident or claim that arises separately from the original case, requiring its own litigation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' according to the Hanbali judge quoted in the passage?
A) Ruling on the contract itself
B) Ruling according to the necessary consequence of the claim
C) Ruling on the validity of the contract
D) Ruling on the pre-emption right

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling on a contract's validity does not automatically rule on other legal consequences; each right must be separately claimed and litigated.

Conditions for Enforcing a Foreign Madhhab Judge's Ruling
Stating the three conditions for a judge's ruling from another madhhab to be enforceable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for accepting a judge's ruling from another madhhab and identify when a ruling contradicts the Quran, well-known Sunnah, or Ijma.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling from another madhhab is void if it contradicts a clear text or established consensus.

A judge in a Hanafi court may encounter a ruling passed by a judge from another madhhab. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sets the boundaries: not every such ruling is automatically enforced. The ruling must pass three tests of compatibility with the fundamental sources of Islamic law. If it fails, it is not a valid ijtihad but a contradiction (khilaf) that must be overturned.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the opening line: 'And a condition is that it must not contradict the Book, the Sunnah, and the consensus.' Note that 'Sunnah' is qualified as 'well-known' (mashhura) — this is important for the Hanafi understanding.
The three sources are Quran, well-known Sunnah, and Ijma (specifically consensus of the Companions on a ruling without valid ikhtilaf).
2
Analyze the Quran contradiction example: meat without tasmiya
The author gives the example of a ruling permitting meat from an animal slaughtered without mentioning Allah's name. The Quran verse (6:121) is cited. Work through the grammatical discussion of the waw in 'wa innahu lafisqun' — the Hanafi interpretation takes it as general, covering any meat without tasmiya.
This example shows that a ruling contradicting the apparent general meaning of the Quran is void.
3
Analyze the Sunnah contradiction example: single witness and oath
The well-known hadith 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier' is cited. A ruling that accepts one witness and an oath contradicts this established procedure.
The hadith is mashhura — widely transmitted and accepted, so it binds all madhhabs.
4
Analyze the Ijma contradiction example: ummahat al-awlad
There is consensus (ijma) that slave mothers of children (ummahat al-awlad) cannot be sold. Any ruling permitting their sale would contradict this consensus and is void.
The author clarifies that ijma here means a ruling on which there is no valid ikhtilaf based on a legal proof.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on meat without tasmiya
The passage notes a strange case from al-Khulasa: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider a ruling allowing such meat to be within ijtihad (and thus acceptable if issued by a Hanafi judge), while Abu Yusuf does not. The author reconciles this by distinguishing the situation before and after a judge's ruling.
This shows that even when a ruling appears to contradict a text, it may be considered ijtihad if the text's meaning is open to interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط أن لا يخالف الكتاب والسنة والإجماع، فإن خالف واحداً منها لم يمضه، وإنما ينقصه لكونه ليس في محل الاجتهاد الصحيح وهو خلاف لا اختلاف، ومثال ما خالف الكتاب القضاء بحل متروك التسمية عامداً لقوله تعالى ولا تأكلوا مما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه [الأنعام: ١٢١] بناء على أنه شامل لذبائح المسلمين كالمشركين بناء على أن الواو في قوله تعالى وأنه لفسق» للعطف، وأما إذا كانت للحال كانت مقيدة بما أهل به لغير الله لأن الفسق فسر كذلك في قوله أو فسقاً أهل به لغير الله ولذا قال في التحرير : إن الواو تحتمل أن تكون حالاً فتكون قيداً للنهي عن أكل ما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه، ويحتمل أن يراد بما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه الميتة أو ما ذكر عليه اسم غير الله تعالى فإن الفسق هو أهل به لغير الله تعالى. ومثال ما خالف السنة أي المشهورة القضاء بشاهد ويمين فإنه مخالف للحديث المشهور البينة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر. (۱) ومثال القضاء المخالف للإجماع القضاء أمهات الأولاد، والمراد من الإجماع ما ليس ببيع فيه خلاف يستند إلى دليل شرعي ومن الغريب ما في الخلاصة وأما القضاء بحل متروك التسمية عامداً فجائز عندهما وعند أبي يوسف لا يجوز ا هـ. وهو يدل على أنه مما يسوغ فيه الاجتهاد عندهما لأنه يفيد الحل كما فهمه ابن الهمام لأنه لا خلاف عندنا في عدم الحل، من قبيل ما لا يسوغ فيه الاجتهاد عندنا لنقل الفقهاء والأصوليين بحيث شددوا والآثار إذ لا يصح الشيء مع تخلف آثاره عنه ، وكذا الحكم بالصحة يتضمن الحكم بالموجب فإذا حكم بصحة الشيء فقد حكم بترتب آثاره عليه . ثم ذكر أن التحقيق أن الحكم بالموجب وارد على الآثار نصاً ومنها الصحة بخلاف الحكم بالصحة فإنه يتناول الآثار ضمناً لا صريحاً فيكون الحكم بالموجب أعلى لتناوله جميع الآثار لأنه مفرد مضاف فيعم كل موجب لكنه خلاف المشهور قوله : (لأنه لا خلاف عندنا في عدم الحل) علة لقوله ومن الغريب والظاهر أنه علة لقوله «لا أنه يفيد الحل الخ». وفيه نظر، فإن عدم الخلاف عندنا في عدم الحل قبل حكم حاكم يحله، أما بعد حكم حاكم يراه ففيه خلاف وهو مانقله في الخلاصة وهذا مراد صاحب الفتح بإفادته الحل فإن ما في الخلاصة دل على أنه مما يسوغ فيه الاجتهاد فإذا كان كذلك أفاد القضاء به الحل كما لا يخفى قوله : (والحق أنه (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الرهن باب ٦ الترمذي في كتاب الأحكام باب ١٢. ابن ماجه في كتاب الأحكام باب ۷ بلفظ فالبينة على المدعي واليمين على المدعى عليه».
And a condition is that it must not contradict the Book (Quran), the Sunnah, and the consensus (ijma). If it contradicts any one of them, he does not enforce it; rather, he deducts it because it is not in the domain of correct ijtihad, and it is a contradiction (khilaf) not a disagreement (ikhtilaf). An example of what contradicts the Quran is a ruling that allows eating meat on which the name of Allah was deliberately omitted, because of His saying 'And do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned' [6:121], based on the interpretation that it is inclusive of the slaughtered animals of Muslims and polytheists alike, because the 'waw' in His saying 'and indeed it is wickedness' is for conjunction. But if it is for 'haal' (circumstantial), then it is restricted to what has been offered to other than Allah, because wickedness has been interpreted thus in His saying 'or a wickedness offered to other than Allah'. Therefore, it was said in al-Tahrir: the 'waw' can be a 'haal' (circumstantial), so it becomes a restriction to the prohibition of eating that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned; and it can be intended by 'what the name of Allah has not been mentioned upon' the carrion or what the name of other than Allah is mentioned upon, because the wickedness is offered to other than Allah. And an example of what contradicts the Sunnah, i.e., the well-known (mashhura), is a ruling based on a testimony and an oath, for it contradicts the well-known hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' (1) And an example of a ruling that contradicts consensus (ijma) is a ruling that the mothers of children (ummahat al-awlad) are not to be sold. And what is meant by consensus here is that which has no difference of opinion that is based on a legal proof. And among the strange things is what is in al-Khulasa: 'As for the ruling that allows eating meat on which the name was deliberately omitted, it is permissible according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), but according to Abu Yusuf it is not permissible.' End quote. And this indicates that it is among those matters in which ijtihad is permissible according to the two, because it yields permissibility as understood by Ibn al-Humann, because there is no disagreement among us on the lack of permissibility (before a judge's ruling); it is among those matters in which ijtihad is not permissible according to us, due to the transfer of the jurists and methodologists to such an extent that they emphasized the effects (athar), for a thing is not valid if its effects are delayed from it. Likewise, the ruling on validity includes the ruling on the necessary consequence; so if one rules on the validity of a thing, he has ruled on the arrangement of its effects upon it. Then he mentioned that the verification is that 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' concerns the effects explicitly, including validity, unlike the ruling on validity, which deals with the effects implicitly, not explicitly. Therefore, 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' is superior because it encompasses all effects, since it is a genitive construction (mudaf + mudaf ilayh) that generalizes every necessary consequence; but it is contrary to the well-known (position). His saying: 'Because there is no disagreement among us on the lack of permissibility' is a reason for his saying 'and it is strange', and apparently it is a reason for his saying 'rather, it yields permissibility etc.' There is consideration in it: for the lack of disagreement among us on the lack of permissibility is before a judge rules on its permissibility; but after a judge rules on it, there is disagreement, and that is what is transmitted in al-Khulasa. And this is what the author of al-Fath intended by its yielding permissibility. For what is in al-Khulasa indicates that it is among matters where ijtihad is permissible; and if that is the case, then the ruling on it yields permissibility, as is not hidden. His saying: 'And the truth is that...' (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in the Book of Pledge, chapter 6; al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Judgments, chapter 12; Ibn Majah in the Book of Judgments, chapter 7 with the wording: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a ruling_with_proof expansion_probe that establishes three conditions for accepting a judge's ruling from another madhhab: it must not contradict the Quran, the well-known Sunnah, or consensus (ijma of the Companions). If it does, it is invalid because it falls outside the realm of valid ijtihad, becoming a 'khilaf' (opposition) rather than a permissible 'ikhtilaf' (difference). The author gives three examples. First, a ruling allowing meat without tasmiya (saying Bismillah) contradicts the Quran verse (6:121). He discusses the grammatical interpretation of the verse, noting that the waw could be conjunctive or circumstantial, affecting the ruling. Second, a ruling based on a single witness and an oath contradicts the famous hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' Third, a ruling that ummahat al-awlad (slave mothers of children) can be sold contradicts consensus (though some differences exist based on valid legal proof). The passage then addresses a peculiar case from al-Khulasa: a ruling allowing meat without tasmiya is considered 'permissible' (jā'iz) by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not by Abu Yusuf. This indicates that Abu Hanifa and Muhammad considered it within the scope of ijtihad, while Abu Yusuf did not. The author reconciles this by noting that before a judge rules, there is no disagreement on the prohibition; but after a judge rules differently, the ruling may be accepted if based on ijtihad. The passage then returns to the concept of 'al-hukm bi al-mujib', arguing that a ruling on validity implicitly includes its effects, while 'al-hukm bi al-mujib' explicitly includes all effects, making it more comprehensive. This analysis shows the sophisticated approach to judicial rulings in Hanafi fiqh: not every ruling from another madhhab is automatically accepted; it must pass the test of compatibility with primary sources.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-kitab): The Quran, as the primary source of law. A ruling contradicting it is invalid.
السنة المشهورة (al-sunnah al-mashhura): A well-known and widely transmitted hadith (not necessarily mutawatir but widely accepted).
الإجماع (al-ijma): Consensus of the Companions (particularly) on a ruling, with no valid difference based on a legal proof.
متروك التسمية (matruk al-tasmiya): Meat of an animal on which Allah's name was deliberately not mentioned at slaughter.
أمهات الأولاد (ummahat al-awlad): Slave women who have borne children to their master; consensus that they cannot be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling from another madhhab is enforceable only if it does not contradict the Quran, the well-known Sunnah, or Ijma that has no valid ikhtilaf.

Conditions for Valid Judicial Rulings: No Contradiction of Primary Sources
Explains a condition for valid judicial rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition that a judge's ruling must not contradict the Qur'an, Sunnah, or Ijma, and apply it to the example of meat without tasmiyah.

Core Idea
A judge's ruling contravening a clear text is invalid and unenforceable.

A judge's ruling carries legal weight only when it conforms to the fundamental sources of Shari'ah. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a critical boundary: if a ruling contradicts the Qur'an, Sunnah, or Ijma, it is not a valid exercise of ijtihad but a clear violation. Through the example of meat slaughtered without mentioning Allah's name, we see how a mistaken grammatical interpretation leads to a ruling that opposes the clear general prohibition of the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Condition
Locate the clause 'وشرط أن لا يخالف الكتاب والسنة والإجماع'. This states a condition for a judge's ruling to be valid: it must not contradict any of the three primary sources.
This is a general principle in Hanafi qada.
2
Understand the Consequence of Violation
The phrase 'فإن خالف واحداً منها لم يمضه' tells us that if the ruling contradicts any one of them, it is not enforced. The author adds that such contradiction is a defect because it is outside the scope of valid ijtihad.
The distinction between 'khilaf' (contradiction) and 'ikhtilaf' (valid difference) is crucial.
3
Analyze the Example Given
The example is a ruling permitting consumption of meat from an animal slaughtered without tasmiyah intentionally. This is said to contradict the Qur'anic verse 6:121. Follow how the verse is interpreted in the Hanafi school: as a general prohibition covering all such meat.
The verse: 'And do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned.'
4
Examine the Grammatical Dispute (Waw)
The text discusses the role of the waw in 'وأنه لفسق'. Two interpretations: (1) conjunctive waw makes the prohibition general; (2) circumstantial waw restricts the prohibition to meat dedicated to other than Allah. The Hanafi school adopts the first view.
Referenced from Al-Tahrir: the waw is probably circumstantial, but the preferred view maintains generality.
5
Conclude the Ruling's Invalidity
Because the verse is clear and general, a ruling that permits such meat contradicts it. Therefore, that ruling is invalid (batil) and not enforceable.
This demonstrates the condition in action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط أن لا يخالف الكتاب والسنة والإجماع، فإن خالف واحداً منها لم يمضه، وإنما ينقصه لكونه ليس في محل الاجتهاد الصحيح وهو خلاف لا اختلاف، ومثال ما خالف الكتاب القضاء بحل متروك التسمية عامداً لقوله تعالى ولا تأكلوا مما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه [الأنعام: ١٢١] بناء على أنه شامل لذبائح المسلمين كالمشركين بناء على أن الواو في قوله تعالى وأنه لفسق» للعطف، وأما إذا كانت للحال كانت مقيدة بما أهل به لغير الله لأن الفسق فسر كذلك في قوله أو فسقاً أهل به لغير الله ولذا قال في التحرير : إن الواو تحتمل أن تكون حالاً فتكون قيداً للنهي عن أكل ما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه، ويحتمل أن يراد بما لم يذكر اسم الله عليه الميتة أو ما ذكر عليه اسم غير الله تعالى فإن الفسق هو أهل به لغير الله تعالى.
And a condition is that it does not contradict the Book, the Sunnah, and the Consensus. If it contradicts any one of them, it is not enforced. Rather, it is defective because it is not in the place of correct ijtihad, and it is a contradiction, not a difference (of opinion). An example of contradicting the Book is a ruling that meat left without mentioning the name of Allah deliberately is permissible, due to His statement: 'And do not eat of that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned' (Al-An'am 121), based on the interpretation that it includes the slaughter of Muslims like polytheists, based on the interpretation that the waw in His statement 'and indeed it is a transgression' is for conjunction. But if it is for the condition of the state, then it is restricted to what is dedicated to other than Allah, because transgression is thus interpreted in His statement 'or a transgression [of the law] upon which the name of other than Allah has been invoked.' Hence it is said in Al-Tahrir: The waw is probably a circumstantial particle, thus it is a restriction on the prohibition of eating that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned, and it is probable that what is meant by 'that upon which the name of Allah has not been mentioned' is carrion or that upon which the name of other than Allah has been invoked, for the transgression is that which is dedicated to other than Allah.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes a fundamental principle in Islamic judicial procedure: a judge's ruling must conform to the primary sources of Shari'ah—the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and consensus (ijma). If a ruling contradicts any of these, it is invalid and must not be enforced. The text emphasizes that such contradiction is not a valid difference of opinion (ikhtilaf) but a clear violation (khilaf). The example given is the ruling of permissibility of eating meat from an animal slaughtered without reciting the basmalah intentionally. The Qur'anic verse from Surah al-An'am (6:121) is cited. The Hanafi school understands this verse as a general prohibition, covering all cases where the name of Allah is not mentioned, regardless of the religion of the slaughterer. The discussion involves the grammatical interpretation of the waw (and) in the following phrase, whether it is conjunctive (wa) or circumstantial (hal), which affects the meaning. According to the conjunctive reading, the prohibition includes all animals not slaughtered with tasmiyah. According to the circumstantial reading, it refers only to animals dedicated to other than Allah. The text references Al-Tahrir for these interpretations. The key point is that a ruling contradicting such a clear text is not subject to ijtihad and is therefore unenforceable.

Key Terms
الكتاب (al-Kitab): The Quran, the primary source of Islamic law.
السنة (al-Sunnah): The prophetic tradition, second primary source.
الإجماع (al-Ijma): Consensus of the mujtahids of the community on a legal ruling.
الاجتهاد (al-ijtihad): Independent legal reasoning by a qualified scholar.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The ruling is valid if it follows another madhhab.
B) The ruling is not enforced and is considered defective.
C) The ruling is enforced but can be appealed.
D) The ruling becomes valid if the judge is a mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
A judge's ruling must conform to the Qur'an, Sunnah, and Ijma; any contradiction renders it invalid and unenforceable.

Example of a Ruling Contradicting the Sunnah: Witness and Oath
Gives an example of a ruling contradicting the well-known sunnah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a ruling that contradicts a well-known hadith and state why it is invalid.

Core Idea
A ruling that contradicts a well-known hadith is invalid, even if based on analogy.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq provides a concrete example of a ruling that contradicts the well-known Sunnah. The ruling under question is a judge accepting a single witness along with the plaintiff's oath as proof. The commentator explains that this directly opposes the famous hadith that assigns the burden of proof to the claimant and the oath to the defendant. Understanding this example helps you see how primary sources are used to invalidate secondary rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
The ruling in question: a judge accepts the testimony of a single witness combined with the plaintiff's oath as sufficient evidence to establish a claim.
This is an example of a ruling that is claimed to contradict the well-known Sunnah.
2
State the hadith that is contradicted
The hadith: 'البينة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر' (The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies). This hadith is widely transmitted (mashhur) and recorded in major collections.
The hadith explicitly assigns the oath to the defendant, not the claimant.
3
Explain the contradiction
The hadith places the oath on the defendant to deny the claim. The ruling under discussion, however, uses the plaintiff's oath as part of the proof, effectively shifting the burden of proof to the defendant. This directly contradicts the explicit wording of the hadith.
The contradiction is clear from the text of the hadith.
4
Determine the consequence
Because the ruling contradicts a well-known hadith, it is invalid (batil) and must not be enforced by a judge. No legitimate ijtihad can produce a ruling that conflicts with an explicit primary source.
The ruling is null and void.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثال ما خالف السنة أي المشهورة القضاء بشاهد ويمين فإنه مخالف للحديث المشهور البينة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر. رواه البخاري في كتاب الرهن باب ٦ والترمذي في كتاب الأحكام باب ١٢ وابن ماجه في كتاب الأحكام باب ۷ بلفظ فالبينة على المدعي واليمين على المدعى عليه.
And an example of what contradicts the Sunnah, i.e., the well-known (sunnah), is a ruling based on a single witness and an oath. For it contradicts the famous hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.' Narrated by Bukhari in the Book of Rahn, chapter 6; al-Tirmidhi in the Book of Judgments, chapter 12; and Ibn Majah in the Book of Judgments, chapter 7, with the wording: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the defendant.'
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage provides the second example of a ruling that contradicts a primary source: the Sunnah. A judge's ruling that accepts the testimony of a single witness combined with the plaintiff's oath as sufficient to establish a claim goes against a well-known hadith. The hadith, which is widely transmitted and accepted, establishes that the plaintiff bears the burden of proof (usually requiring two witnesses or one witness and a corroborating oath? Actually the hadith states the burden of proof is on the claimant, and the defendant is required to take an oath to deny the claim. The ruling that a single witness plus an oath is enough contradicts this principle. Therefore, such a ruling is not from legitimate ijtihad and must not be enforced. The hadith is cited from Bukhari, Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah.

Key Terms
السنة المشهورة (al-Sunnah al-mashhurah): A widely known and transmitted hadith, though not necessarily mutawatir, but well-known among scholars.
شاهد ويمين (shahid wa yamin): A form of evidence consisting of one witness and an oath by the plaintiff.
البينة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر (al-bayyinatu 'ala man idda'a wa al-yaminu 'ala man ankara): The hadith that establishes the burden of proof on the claimant and the oath on the defendant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What type of evidence does the hadith 'البينة على من ادعى واليمين على من أنكر' require?
A) The claimant must provide a witness and the defendant takes an oath.
B) The claimant must provide proof, and the defendant takes an oath to deny.
C) The claimant and defendant both swear oaths.
D) Only the claimant needs to produce a witness.

Key Takeaway
Any ruling that contradicts a well-known hadith is invalid; therefore, a judgment based on a single witness and the plaintiff's oath is batil.
